| **Thus** | Formal; "as a result" | *The road was icy. Thus, driving was hazardous.* |
| **As a result** | Direct consequence | *She studied diligently. As a result, she passed the exam.* |
| **Hence** | Formal; "for this reason" | *He was caught cheating; hence, he was disqualified.* |
| **Accordingly** | In accordance with what came before | *The weather was severe; accordingly, the event was postponed.* |

#### Similarity Transitions

These signal that the upcoming information parallels or resembles the previous information.

| Transition | Example |
|---|---|
| **Similarly** | *She excelled in mathematics. Similarly, her brother was a math prodigy.* |
| **Likewise** | *He contributed to the project. Likewise, his colleagues put in extra effort.* |
| **In the same way** | *The first experiment succeeded. In the same way, the second experiment yielded positive results.* |
| **By the same token** | *We should be kind to our friends. By the same token, we should be kind to strangers.* |

#### Concession Transitions

These signal that the author acknowledges a counterpoint or opposing fact before making their main argument.

| Transition | Example |
|---|---|
| **Granted** | *Granted, the plan is risky. However, the potential rewards are enormous.* |
| **Although** | *Although the plan is risky, the potential rewards are enormous.* |
| **Despite / In spite of** | *Despite the risks, they proceeded with the plan.* |
| **Even so** | *The evidence was circumstantial. Even so, the jury convicted.* |
| **Regardless** | *Regardless of the obstacles, she persevered.* |

#### Sequence / Order Transitions

These signal chronological or logical order.

| Transition | Example |
|---|---|
| **First, second, third** | *First, gather the ingredients. Second, mix them together. Third, bake for 30 minutes.* |
| **Next / Then** | *She finished her homework. Next, she prepared dinner.* |
| **Subsequently** | *The company launched the product. Subsequently, sales increased dramatically.* |
| **Eventually** | *After many attempts, she eventually succeeded.* |
| **Finally** | *Finally, the committee reached a decision.* |
| **Previously** | *Previously, this task required manual labor.* |
| **Before / After** | *Before the meeting, review the agenda. After the meeting, submit your report.* |

#### Conclusion Transitions

These signal that the author is summarizing or drawing a final conclusion.

| Transition | Example |
|---|---|
| **In conclusion** | *In conclusion, the evidence supports the hypothesis.* |
| **To sum up** | *To sum up, three main factors contributed to the outcome.* |
| **In summary** | *In summary, the project was a success.* |
| **In other words** | *The plan is unworkable. In other words, it will fail.* |
| **Ultimately** | *Ultimately, the decision rests with the board.* |

---

## 2.16 — Prepositional Phrases: The Great Distractors

A **prepositional phrase** consists of a preposition followed by its object (a noun or pronoun) and any modifiers of that object.

**Structure:** Preposition + Object of the Preposition (+ modifiers)

**Examples:**

- *in the house*
- *with great enthusiasm*
- *under the old oak tree*
- *after the long meeting*
- *between you and me*

### Why Prepositional Phrases Matter

Prepositional phrases are the primary tool test-makers use to obscure the subject of a sentence. Because the subject is NEVER found within a prepositional phrase, identifying and mentally eliminating prepositional phrases is the most effective strategy for solving subject-verb agreement problems.

**Example:**

- *The collection of rare manuscripts **is** housed in the library.*

If you mistakenly identify "manuscripts" as the subject (because it's closest to the verb), you might incorrectly choose "are." But "of rare manuscripts" is a prepositional phrase. The true subject is "collection" (singular), so the verb must be "is."

### Common Prepositions

| Category | Prepositions |
|---|---|
| **Time** | at, on, in, during, before, after, since, until, by, for, within |
| **Place** | at, on, in, under, over, above, below, between, among, beside, behind, in front of, near, far from |
| **Direction** | to, from, into, out of, toward, through, across, along, up, down |
| **Manner** | by, with, in, on, like, as |
| **Cause/Purpose** | because of, due to, for, in order to |
| **Other** | of, about, regarding, concerning, despite, except, without, with, including |

### The "Cross-Out" Strategy

When analyzing a sentence for subject-verb agreement or sentence structure:

1. **Identify all prepositional phrases.**
2. **Cross them out mentally or on paper.**
3. **Find the subject and verb in what remains.**
4. **Check for agreement.**

**Example:**

- *The impact of these changes on the overall economy **has** been significant.*

Cross out: "of these changes" and "on the overall economy."
What remains: "The impact has been significant."
Subject: "impact" (singular) → Verb: "has" (singular) → Correct.

---

## 2.17 — Sentence Fragments: The Incomplete Sentence

A **sentence fragment** is a group of words that is punctuated as a sentence but is not actually a complete sentence. It may lack a subject, a verb, or a complete thought.

### Types of Fragments

#### Type 1: Dependent Clause Standing Alone

- *Because she was tired.* (This is a dependent clause. It cannot stand alone.)

**Fix:** Attach it to an independent clause.
- *Because she was tired, she went to bed early.*

#### Type 2: Participial Phrase Standing Alone

- *Walking down the street.* (No subject, no complete verb.)

**Fix:** Add a subject and a complete verb.
- *Walking down the street, she noticed the new café.*

#### Type 3: Fragment Missing a Subject

- *Went to the store.* (Who went? No subject.)

**Fix:** Add a subject.
- *She went to the store.*

#### Type 4: Fragment Missing a Main Verb

- *The man in the blue coat.* (What did he do? No verb.)

**Fix:** Add a verb.
- *The man in the blue coat entered the building.*

### How to Identify Fragments

1. **Check for a subject.** (Who or what is the sentence about?)
2. **Check for a main verb.** (What is the subject doing or being?)
3. **Check for a complete thought.** (Does the sentence express a complete idea, or does it leave you hanging?)

If any of these elements is missing, you have a fragment.

---

## 2.18 — Parallel Structure: The Rule of Consistent Form

**Parallel structure** (also called **parallelism**) requires that elements in a sentence that serve the same function must have the same grammatical form.

### Parallelism in Lists

When listing items, all items must be in the same grammatical form.

**Incorrect:**
- *She likes swimming, to run, and biking.* (Gerund, infinitive, gerund—not parallel.)

**Correct:**
- *She likes swimming, running, and biking.* (All gerunds.)
- OR: *She likes to swim, to run, and to bike.* (All infinitives.)

### Parallelism with Correlative Conjunctions

Correlative conjunctions come in pairs: *either...or, neither...nor, not only...but also, both...and, whether...or.*

The grammatical structure after each part of the pair must be identical.

**Incorrect:**
- *She not only is intelligent but also has kindness.* (Adjective after "not only," verb phrase after "but also.")

**Correct:**
- *She is not only intelligent but also kind.* (Adjective after both parts.)

**Incorrect:**
- *He either wants to go to the movies or staying home.* (Infinitive after "either," gerund after "or.")

**Correct:**
- *He wants either to go to the movies or to stay home.* (Infinitive after both parts.)

### Parallelism in Comparisons

**Incorrect:**
- *Running a marathon is harder than to swim a mile.*

**Correct:**
- *Running a marathon is harder than swimming a mile.* (Gerund compared to gerund.)

### Parallelism in Verb Tenses

**Incorrect:**
- *She finished her homework, ate dinner, and then she was watching TV.*

**Correct:**
- *She finished her homework, ate dinner, and then watched TV.* (All simple past.)

---

## 2.19 — Pronoun Rules: Agreement, Reference, and Case

### Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement

A pronoun must agree in number (singular/plural) and gender (masculine/feminine/neuter) with its antecedent (the noun it refers to).

**Incorrect:**
- *Each student must bring their textbook.* ("Each student" is singular; "their" is plural.)

**Correct:**
- *Each student must bring his or her textbook.*
- OR (in modern usage, accepted by many style guides): *Each student must bring their textbook.* (Singular "they" is increasingly accepted but may be tested as incorrect on some standardized exams.)

**Incorrect:**
- *The team won its first game.* (If "team" is treated as a collective singular, "its" is correct. But if the emphasis is on individual members: *The team won their first games.*)

### Pronoun Reference Clarity

A pronoun must clearly refer to one specific antecedent. Ambiguous pronoun reference occurs when it's unclear which noun the pronoun refers to.

**Ambiguous:**
- *John told Mark that he had made a mistake.* (Who made the mistake—John or Mark?)

**Clear:**
- *John told Mark, "I have made a mistake."* (Direct quote eliminates ambiguity.)
- OR: *John admitted to Mark that he himself had made a mistake.* (Clarifies that John made the mistake.)

### Pronoun Case

Pronouns change form depending on their function in the sentence:

| Case | Pronouns | Function |
|---|---|---|
| **Subjective (Nominative)** | I, you, he, she, it, we, they | Subject of a verb |
| **Objective** | me, you, him, her, it, us, them | Object of a verb or preposition |
| **Possessive** | my/mine, your/yours, his, her/hers, its, our/ours, their/theirs | Ownership |

**Common errors:**

- *Me and him went to the store.* (Incorrect—should be subjective case: *He and I went to the store.*)
- *Between you and I, this is a bad idea.* (Incorrect—"between" is a preposition, so it takes the objective case: *Between you and me.*)

**Quick test for compound subjects/objects:** Remove the other person and see if the pronoun sounds correct.

- *Me went to the store.* → Clearly wrong. *I went to the store.* → Correct. So: *He and I went to the store.*
- *Between I* → Clearly wrong. *Between me* → Correct. So: *Between you and me.*

---

## 2.20 — Advanced Punctuation: Quotation Marks, Parentheses, and Hyphens

### Quotation Marks

#### In American English:

- **Double quotation marks** (" ") are used for direct quotes.
- **Single quotation marks** (' ') are used for quotes within quotes.

- *She said, "I can't believe he called me 'unprofessional.'"*

#### Punctuation with Quotation Marks (American Rules):

- **Commas and periods** go **inside** quotation marks.
  - *She said, "I'll be there."*
  - *"I'll be there," she said.*

- **Colons and semicolons** go **outside** quotation marks.
  - *She called it "a disaster"; I disagreed.*

- **Question marks and exclamation points** go **inside** if they are part of the quote, **outside** if they are not.
  - *She asked, "Are you coming?"* (The question is part of the quote.)
  - *Did she really say, "I quit"?* (The question is the writer's, not the speaker's.)

#### Quotation Marks for Titles:

- Use quotation marks for short works: articles, short stories, poems, songs, TV episodes, chapters.
  - *I read the article "The Future of AI" in The New Yorker.*

### Parentheses

Parentheses enclose supplementary or explanatory material that is not essential to the main sentence.

- *The study (conducted over a five-year period) yielded significant results.*

**Punctuation with parentheses:**
- If the parenthetical material is within a sentence, the period goes outside the closing parenthesis.
  - *She finally arrived (after a two-hour delay).*
- If the parenthetical material is a complete sentence standing alone, the period goes inside.
  - *She finally arrived. (She had been delayed for two hours.)*

### Hyphens

Hyphens join compound words and prefixes.

**Use hyphens for:**
- Compound adjectives before a noun: *a well-known author, a two-year-old child*
- Prefixes before proper nouns: *mid-July, anti-American*
- Numbers twenty-one through ninety-nine: *forty-two, seventy-six*
- Fractions used as adjectives: *a two-thirds majority*

**Do NOT use hyphens for:**
- Compound adjectives after a noun: *The author is well known.*
- Adverbs ending in -ly: *a highly respected scholar*

---

## 2.21 — The Subjunctive Mood: Hypothetical and Contrary-to-Fact

The **subjunctive mood** is used to express wishes, demands, suggestions, and conditions contrary to fact.

### Type 1: "That" Clauses After Verbs of Demand/Suggestion

After verbs like *demand, suggest, insist, recommend, require, ask, propose, urge*, the subjunctive uses the **base form of the verb** (no -s, no tense change).

- *She demanded that he **leave** immediately.* (Not "leaves" or "left.")
- *I suggest that she **study** for the exam.* (Not "studies.")
- *The doctor recommended that he **avoid** strenuous exercise.* (Not "avoids.")

### Type 2: Contrary-to-Fact Conditions (If Clauses)

When expressing a condition that is contrary to fact (unreal), use "were" instead of "was" for all subjects (including singular).

- *If I **were** you, I would accept the offer.* (Not "If I was you.")
- *If she **were** here, she would know what to do.* (Not "If she was here.")

**Note:** If the condition is possible (not contrary to fact), use the indicative mood:
- *If she **was** at the meeting, I didn't see her.* (It's possible she was there—we don't know.)

### Type 3: Wishes

- *I wish I **were** taller.* (Not "I wish I was taller.")
- *She wishes she **had studied** harder.* (Past subjunctive for a past wish.)

---

## 2.22 — Active vs. Passive Voice: Clarity and Emphasis

### Active Voice

In active voice, the subject performs the action.

- *The committee **approved** the proposal.*

### Passive Voice

In passive voice, the subject receives the action. The agent (the performer) may be omitted or introduced with "by."

- *The proposal **was approved** (by the committee).*

### When to Use Each

- **Active voice** is generally preferred for its clarity, directness, and energy.
- **Passive voice** is appropriate when:
  - The agent is unknown or unimportant: *The bank was robbed.*
  - The focus should be on the recipient of the action: *The president was sworn in yesterday.*
  - Scientific writing (traditionally): *The solution was heated to 100°C.*

### How to Identify Passive Voice

A passive construction always contains:
1. A form of the verb "be" (am, is, are, was, were, been, being)
2. A past participle

- *The report **was written** by the analyst.* (Passive)
- *The analyst **wrote** the report.* (Active)

---

## 2.23 — Sentence Structure Patterns: The Five Basic Types

English sentences can be classified into five basic structural patterns based on their clause composition.

### Type 1: Simple Sentence (One IC, No DC)

- *The cat slept.*
- *The students completed their assignments on time.*

A simple sentence may have compound subjects or compound verbs, but it contains only one independent clause.

### Type 2: Compound Sentence (Two or More ICs)

- *The cat slept, and the dog barked.*
- *She finished her work; she went home.*

Joined by: comma + FANBOYS, semicolon, semicolon + conjunctive adverb, or dash.

### Type 3: Complex Sentence (One IC + One or More DCs)

- *Although it was raining, we went for a walk.*
- *The book that I borrowed was fascinating.*

### Type 4: Compound-Complex Sentence (Two or More ICs + One or More DCs)

- *Although it was raining, we went for a walk, and we enjoyed it.*
- *The book that I borrowed was fascinating, so I read it in one sitting.*

### Type 5: Fragment (Incomplete)

- *Because she was tired.* (DC alone—not a sentence.)
- *Running down the street.* (Participial phrase—not a sentence.)

---

## 2.24 — Common Grammatical Errors: A Comprehensive Reference

### Error 1: Comma Splice

- *She was tired, she went to bed early.* (Incorrect)
- *She was tired, so she went to bed early.* (Correct—comma + FANBOYS)
- *She was tired; she went to bed early.* (Correct—semicolon)
- *She was tired. She went to bed early.* (Correct—period)

### Error 2: Run-on (Fused Sentence)

- *She was tired she went to bed early.* (Incorrect—no punctuation)
- *She was tired, so she went to bed early.* (Correct)

### Error 3: Subject-Verb Disagreement

- *The list of items are on the desk.* (Incorrect—"list" is the subject, not "items.")
- *The list of items is on the desk.* (Correct)

### Error 4: Pronoun-Antecedent Disagreement

- *Every student must bring their book.* (Incorrect on formal tests—"every student" is singular.)
- *Every student must bring his or her book.* (Correct)

### Error 5: Dangling Modifier

- *Walking to school, the rain started.* (Incorrect—the rain wasn't walking.)
- *Walking to school, I was caught in the rain.* (Correct)

### Error 6: Misplaced Modifier

- *She almost drove her kids to school every day.* (Incorrect—ambiguous.)
- *She drove her kids to school almost every day.* (Correct)

### Error 7: Faulty Parallelism

- *She likes swimming, to run, and biking.* (Incorrect)
- *She likes swimming, running, and biking.* (Correct)

### Error 8: Incorrect Pronoun Case

- *Me and him went to the store.* (Incorrect)
- *He and I went to the store.* (Correct)

### Error 9: Its vs. It's

- *Its a beautiful day.* (Incorrect—should be "It's" = "It is.")
- *The dog wagged it's tail.* (Incorrect—should be "its," possessive.)

### Error 10: Who vs. Whom

- *Who did you give the book to?* (Incorrect—should be "whom," object of preposition.)
- *Whom did you give the book to?* (Correct—or more formally: *To whom did you give the book?*)

### Error 11: Then vs. Than

- *She is taller then her brother.* (Incorrect—"then" refers to time.)
- *She is taller than her brother.* (Correct—"than" is for comparison.)

### Error 12: Affect vs. Effect

- *The weather had a significant affect on attendance.* (Incorrect—"affect" is a verb; "effect" is a noun.)
- *The weather had a significant effect on attendance.* (Correct—noun.)
- *The weather affected attendance significantly.* (Correct—verb.)

### Error 13: Fewer vs. Less

- *There are less people here today.* (Incorrect—"people" is countable.)
- *There are fewer people here today.* (Correct—"fewer" for countable nouns.)
- *There is less water in the bottle.* (Correct—"less" for uncountable nouns.)

### Error 14: I vs. Me in Compound Constructions

- *Between you and I, this is wrong.* (Incorrect—"between" takes objective case.)
- *Between you and me, this is wrong.* (Correct)

- *She gave the report to John and I.* (Incorrect—object of preposition "to.")
- *She gave the report to John and me.* (Correct)

---

## 2.25 — Reading Comprehension and Sentence Structure: The Connection

Understanding sentence structure is not merely an academic exercise—it is the foundation of reading comprehension. When you encounter a complex sentence in a passage, your ability to parse its structure determines whether you understand the author's meaning.

### Strategy: Cross Out the Noise

When reading a complex sentence:

1. **Identify and cross out** (mentally or on paper) all prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and comma clauses.
2. **Find the core:** Subject + Verb + Object/Complement.
3. **Determine the logical relationships** between the remaining clauses using transition words and punctuation.

**Example:**

- *The hypothesis, which had been developed over several years of meticulous research and which many of her colleagues had initially dismissed as unworkable, was ultimately confirmed by the experimental data.*

**Step 1: Cross out nonessential material.**
- Cross out: "which had been developed over several years of meticulous research and which many of her colleagues had initially dismissed as unworkable" (nonrestrictive relative clause)

**Step 2: Find the core.**
- *The hypothesis was ultimately confirmed by the experimental data.*

**Step 3: Reintegrate the nonessential information.**
- The hypothesis (developed over years of research, initially dismissed) was confirmed by the data.

This strategy allows you to extract the essential meaning of even the most convoluted sentences.

### The Role of Transitions in Reading Comprehension

Transitions are the author's road signs. They tell you:
- **Contrast:** "However, the results were unexpected." (The next idea will contradict the previous one.)
- **Addition:** "Furthermore, the study revealed..." (The next idea will support the previous one.)
- **Cause/Effect:** "Consequently, the policy was revised." (The next idea is a result.)
- **Conclusion:** "In summary, the evidence suggests..." (The author is wrapping up.)

When you encounter a transition word, **focus on the information that follows it.** That is where the author's real point lies.

---

## 2.26 — Summary: The Master Framework for Sentence Analysis

To analyze any sentence in English, follow this systematic framework:

### Step 1: Identify All Clauses
- Find every subject-verb pair.
- Each pair constitutes a clause.

### Step 2: Classify Each Clause
- Is it independent (can stand alone) or dependent (cannot stand alone)?
- If dependent, what type? (Relative clause, adverbial clause, noun clause?)

### Step 3: Identify the Punctuation Between Clauses
- Period? Semicolon? Comma? Dash? Conjunction?
- Is the punctuation correct for the clause types involved?

### Step 4: Check Subject-Verb Agreement
- Find the true subject (ignore prepositional phrases, relative clauses, comma clauses).
- Ensure the verb agrees in number.

### Step 5: Check Modifiers
- Are all modifiers clearly and logically attached to the words they describe?
- Are there any dangling or misplaced modifiers?

### Step 6: Check Pronoun Reference and Agreement
- Does every pronoun have a clear antecedent?
- Does it agree in number and gender?

### Step 7: Check Parallel Structure
- Are all listed items in the same grammatical form?
- Are correlative conjunctions followed by parallel structures?

### Step 8: Check Verb Tense Consistency
- Are the tenses logical and consistent?
- Are perfect tenses used correctly?

### Step 9: Check Transitions
- Do transition words accurately reflect the logical relationship between ideas?
- Are "furthermore" and "moreover" used correctly (not redundantly)?

### Step 10: Read the Sentence Aloud (Mentally)
- Does it sound natural?
- Does it convey a clear, unambiguous meaning?

---

This chapter has provided a comprehensive, rigorous framework for understanding English grammar at the deepest level. Mastery of these concepts—clauses, punctuation, subject-verb agreement, modifiers, transitions, and sentence structure—is not merely about avoiding errors. It is about developing the ability to construct clear, sophisticated, and persuasive sentences, and to decode the most complex sentences you will encounter in academic and professional reading. The rules are precise, but they form a coherent system. Once you internalize that system, every sentence becomes a puzzle you can solve.

---

# Chapter 3: Advanced Mathematics — Linear Functions, Quadratics, and Algebraic Reasoning

---

## Part I: Linear Functions — The Architecture of Straight Lines

### 3.1 What Is a Linear Function?

A linear function is any function that can be written in the form:

$$y = mx + b$$

where $m$ and $b$ are constants, $x$ is the independent variable, and $y$ is the dependent variable. The graph of any linear function is a **straight line** in the Cartesian coordinate plane. This is the single most fundamental object in all of algebra, and every sub-topic in this chapter is an exploration of its properties, its algebraic representations, its graphical behavior, and its applications to modeling real-world phenomena.

The word "linear" comes from the Latin *linearis*, meaning "pertaining to a line." A linear function is characterized by one essential property: it has a **constant rate of change**. This means that for any equal increase in the input $x$, the output $y$ changes by the same fixed amount every single time. This constant rate of change is what we call the **slope**.

Before we proceed into deep analysis, let us establish the foundational vocabulary with absolute precision:

- **Independent variable ($x$)**: The variable you choose or control. It is the input.
- **Dependent variable ($y$)**: The variable that responds to changes in $x$. It is the output.
- **Slope ($m$)**: The rate at which $y$ changes with respect to $x$. It is the "steepness" of the line.
- **Y-intercept ($b$)**: The value of $y$ when $x = 0$. It is where the line crosses the y-axis.
- **X-intercept**: The value of $x$ when $y = 0$. Also called a **root** or **zero** of the function.
- **Domain**: The set of all permissible $x$-values. For a linear function, unless restricted by context, the domain is all real numbers: $(-\infty, \infty)$.
- **Range**: The set of all resulting $y$-values. For a non-horizontal linear function, the range is all real numbers: $(-\infty, \infty)$. For a horizontal line $y = k$, the range is simply $\{k\}$.

---

### 3.2 The Slope: Rate of Change in Its Purest Form

#### 3.2.1 Definition and Formula

The slope of a line passing through two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$ is defined as:

$$m = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1} = \frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x} = \frac{\text{rise}}{\text{run}}$$

where $\Delta$ (the Greek letter delta) means "change in." The slope tells you exactly how many units the line rises (or falls) for each unit it moves to the right.

**Critical nuance about ordering**: It does not matter which point you call $(x_1, y_1)$ and which you call $(x_2, y_2)$, as long as you are consistent. If you subtract $y_2 - y_1$ in the numerator, you must subtract $x_2 - x_1$ in the denominator. Both subtractions must follow the same "starting point." If you reverse the order in only one place, you will get the wrong sign. Consider this example: if the two points are $(3, 7)$ and $(1, 3)$:

- Correct: $m = \frac{7 - 3}{3 - 1} = \frac{4}{2} = 2$
- Also correct: $m = \frac{3 - 7}{1 - 3} = \frac{-4}{-2} = 2$
- Incorrect: $m = \frac{7 - 3}{1 - 3} = \frac{4}{-2} = -2$ (wrong!)

#### 3.2.2 Interpreting Slope Sign and Magnitude

The sign of the slope tells you the direction of the line:

- **Positive slope ($m > 0$)**: The line rises from left to right. As $x$ increases, $y$ increases. This represents a **direct relationship**.
- **Negative slope ($m < 0$)**: The line falls from left to right. As $x$ increases, $y$ decreases. This represents an **inverse relationship**.
- **Zero slope ($m = 0$)**: The line is horizontal. $y$ does not change regardless of changes in $x$. The line is of the form $y = k$ for some constant $k$.
- **Undefined slope**: The line is vertical. $x$ does not change regardless of changes in $y$. The line is of the form $x = k$ for some constant $k$. **A vertical line is NOT a function** (it fails the vertical line test), but it is still a line.

The magnitude (absolute value) of the slope tells you how steep the line is:

- $|m| > 1$: Steep line (rises more than 1 unit for each 1 unit of horizontal movement)
- $|m| = 1$: Moderate slope at exactly 45° (rises exactly 1 unit for each 1 unit right)
- $0 < |m| < 1$: Gradual/flat line (rises less than 1 unit for each 1 unit right)

#### 3.2.3 Slope in Context: Units and Real-World Meaning

In applied problems, the slope carries units. It is always expressed as:

$$\text{slope} = \frac{\text{units of } y}{\text{units of } x}$$

For instance, if $x$ represents time in hours and $y$ represents distance in miles, then the slope has units of **miles per hour** — this is speed. If $x$ represents the number of units produced and $y$ represents total cost in dollars, the slope is the **marginal cost per unit** in dollars per unit.

This is a crucial point for test-taking: **the slope is always the rate of change of the dependent variable per unit change of the independent variable**. Any time a question asks "how fast is something changing?" or "what is the rate?" in a linear context, you are looking for the slope.

---

### 3.3 Forms of a Linear Equation

There are three principal ways to write the equation of a line. Each form is useful in different circumstances, and you must be fluent in converting between all three.

#### 3.3.1 Slope-Intercept Form: $y = mx + b$

This is the most commonly taught form and is immediately informative: the slope $m$ and the y-intercept $b$ are directly visible in the equation.

- **When to use it**: When you know the slope and the y-intercept; when you want to quickly identify slope and y-intercept; when writing the equation of a line given a graph.
- **How to graph it from this form**: Plot the y-intercept $(0, b)$, then use the slope to find a second point. If $m = \frac{p}{q}$, move $q$ units horizontally (right if positive, left if negative) and $p$ units vertically (up if positive, down if negative) from the y-intercept to locate a second point. Draw the line through both points.

**Example**: $y = \frac{2}{3}x - 4$
- Slope = $\frac{2}{3}$ (rise 2, run 3)
- Y-intercept = $(0, -4)$
- To graph: Start at $(0, -4)$, move 3 right and 2 up to reach $(3, -2)$, connect.

#### 3.3.2 Point-Slope Form: $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$

This form is centered on a specific known point $(x_1, y_1)$ on the line and the slope $m$.

- **When to use it**: When you know the slope and one point on the line (but not the y-intercept). This is incredibly useful because many problems give you a slope and a point rather than a slope and the y-intercept.
- **Derivation**: If the line passes through $(x_1, y_1)$ with slope $m$, then by the slope formula applied to $(x_1, y_1)$ and any general point $(x, y)$ on the line:

$$m = \frac{y - y_1}{x - x_1}$$

Multiplying both sides by $(x - x_1)$:

$$y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$$

- **Critical note**: There is a specific sign structure here. It is $y - y_1$ and $x - x_1$, using subtraction. If your known point is $(-3, 5)$, then the equation becomes $y - 5 = m(x - (-3)) = m(x + 3)$. Students frequently make sign errors substituting negative coordinates into point-slope form. Always remember: the formula uses minus signs, so a negative coordinate becomes a double negative (i.e., a positive).

#### 3.3.3 Standard Form: $Ax + By = C$

In this form, $A$, $B$, and $C$ are integers (not fractions or decimals), and conventionally $A$ is **positive**. Both variables appear on one side of the equation and the constant appears on the other.

- **When to use it**: When setting up systems of equations; when both intercepts are needed quickly; for clarity in certain word problems; when the problem explicitly asks for it.
- **Finding slope from standard form**: Rearrange into slope-intercept form, or use the formula $m = -\frac{A}{B}$.
- **Finding intercepts from standard form**:
  - Let $x = 0$ to find the y-intercept: $By = C \Rightarrow y = C/B$
  - Let $y = 0$ to find the x-intercept: $Ax = C \Rightarrow x = C/A$

**Conversion between forms**: Any linear equation can be algebraically manipulated into any of the three forms using basic equation-solving techniques (adding, subtracting, multiplying, dividing both sides by the same non-zero quantity).

**Example**: Convert $3x - 4y = 12$ to slope-intercept form.
$$-4y = -3x + 12$$
$$y = \frac{3}{4}x - 3$$

The slope is $\frac{3}{4}$ and the y-intercept is $(0, -3)$.

**Special cases for standard form**:
- Horizontal line: $0x + 1y = k$, or simply $y = k$
- Vertical line: $1x + 0y = k$, or simply $x = k$

---

### 3.4 Horizontal and Vertical Lines

These are the two special cases that do not fit perfectly into $y = mx + b$ (vertical lines are not functions at all):

- **Horizontal line**: $y = k$
  - Slope = $0$
  - The line passes through all points where $y = k$, regardless of the x-coordinate
  - Every point has the form $(x, k)$
  - No rise, only run

- **Vertical line**: $x = k$
  - Slope = **undefined** (not zero — this is a common error)
  - The line passes through all points where $x = k$, regardless of the y-coordinate
  - Every point has the form $(k, y)$
  - All rise, no run
  - **Not a function**: a single $x$-value maps to infinitely many $y$-values

---

### 3.5 Parallel and Perpendicular Lines

Understanding the relationship between the slopes of parallel and perpendicular lines is one of the most frequently tested concepts in all of algebra.

#### 3.5.1 Parallel Lines

Two lines are **parallel** if and only if they have **the same slope** and different y-intercepts.

$$m_1 = m_2 \quad \text{and} \quad b_1 \neq b_2$$

Geometrically, parallel lines never intersect because they rise and fall at the same rate — they maintain a constant distance from each other forever.

**Key reasoning**: If two lines have the same slope but also happen to have the same y-intercept, they are not parallel — they are the **same line**. Lines that coincide are called **coincident** or **dependent**. We will discuss this further in the section on systems of equations.

**Example**: The lines $y = 3x + 2$ and $y = 3x - 5$ are parallel because both have slope $3$ but different y-intercepts ($2$ vs. $-5$).

**In standard form**: Two lines $A_1x + B_1y = C_1$ and $A_2x + B_2y = C_2$ are parallel if $\frac{A_1}{A_2} = \frac{B_1}{B_2} \neq \frac{C_1}{C_2}$ (the ratios of the $x$ and $y$ coefficients are equal, but the ratio of the constants is different).

#### 3.5.2 Perpendicular Lines

Two lines are **perpendicular** if and only if their slopes are **negative reciprocals** of each other. This means:

$$m_1 \cdot m_2 = -1$$

Equivalently, $m_2 = -\frac{1}{m_1}$.

Geometrically, perpendicular lines intersect at a **right angle** ($90^\circ$).

**Examples**:
- If one line has slope $3$, a perpendicular line has slope $-\frac{1}{3}$.
- If one line has slope $\frac{2}{5}$, a perpendicular line has slope $-\frac{5}{2}$.
- If one line has slope $-4$, a perpendicular line has slope $\frac{1}{4}$.

**Special case — horizontal and vertical lines**: The negative reciprocal relationship still holds conceptually. A horizontal line has slope $0$. A vertical line has undefined slope. These two are perpendicular. You can think of this as: $0$ and "undefined" represent infinitely steep reciprocals that meet at $90^\circ$.

**In standard form**: Two lines $A_1x + B_1y = C_1$ and $A_2x + B_2y = C_2$ are perpendicular if $A_1A_2 + B_1B_2 = 0$ (or equivalently, $\frac{A_1}{B_1} = -\frac{B_2}{A_2}$).

#### 3.5.3 Finding the Equation of a Parallel or Perpendicular Line

This is one of the most commonly tested skills in algebra. The strategy is:

1. **Identify the slope** $m$ of the given line (you may need to first convert to slope-intercept form to find it).
2. **Determine the slope of the new line**:
   - If parallel: the slope stays the same ($m_{\text{new}} = m_{\text{given}}$)
   - If perpendicular: take the negative reciprocal ($m_{\text{new}} = -1/m_{\text{given}}$)
3. **Use point-slope form** $y - y_1 = m_{\text{new}}(x - x_1)$ with the given point $(x_1, y_1)$ of the new line.
4. If needed, convert to the requested form (slope-intercept or standard form).

---

### 3.6 X-Intercepts and Zeros of Linear Functions

The **x-intercept** (also called a **zero** or **root**) of a function is the point where the line crosses the x-axis, meaning $y = 0$. To find it, set $y = 0$ and solve for $x$:

For $y = mx + b$:
$$0 = mx + b$$
$$mx = -b$$
$$x = -\frac{b}{m}$$

The x-intercept is at $\left(-\frac{b}{m}, 0\right)$.

**Note**: If $m = 0$ and $b \neq 0$, the line is horizontal and never crosses the x-axis (no x-intercept). If $m = 0$ and $b = 0$, the line IS the x-axis (infinitely many x-intercepts).

**In context**: The x-intercept often represents a meaningful "break-even" point, the point where something reaches zero, or an initial condition minus some depletion over time.

---

### 3.7 Graphing Linear Functions — A Complete Strategy

When graphing a linear function by hand, you have several reliable approaches:

**Method 1: Using slope-intercept form**
1. Identify and plot the y-intercept $(0, b)$ on the y-axis.
2. Use the slope $m = \frac{\text{rise}}{\text{run}}$ to locate a second point by moving from the y-intercept.
3. If needed, repeat the slope movement to locate additional points.
4. Connect the points with a straight line extending in both directions (with arrows).

**Method 2: Using a table of values**
1. Choose at least two $x$-values (three is safer to catch errors).
2. Compute the corresponding $y$-values.
3. Plot each $(x, y)$ pair.
4. Connect them with a straight line.

**Method 3: Using intercepts**
1. Find the y-intercept by setting $x = 0$.
2. Find the x-intercept by setting $y = 0$.
3. Plot both intercepts and connect with a straight line.

![General Knowledge Quiz Questions With Answers](https://www.quizony.com/general-knowledge-quiz/imageForSharing.png)

*Note: While this image primarily serves general knowledge content, the visual representation of data plotting is a universal skill applicable to graphing linear functions and all mathematical visualization.*

**Accuracy checks**:
- If your slope is positive, your line should rise from left to right.
- If your slope is negative, your line should fall from left to right.
- If your slope is zero, your line should be horizontal.
- Every point on your line should satisfy the equation when you substitute its coordinates in.
- A ruler or straightedge should be used for physical graphing; on tests, freehand is acceptable if the line is clearly straight.

---

### 3.8 Linear Modeling — Applying Linear Functions to Real-World Contexts

One of the most powerful applications of linear functions is **modeling**: using linear equations to represent and predict real-world behavior. Test questions in this area typically describe a scenario involving a constant rate of change and ask you to construct, interpret, or apply a linear model.

#### 3.8.1 The Modeling Process

1. **Identify the variables**:
   - $x$ = independent variable (the input — often time, but can be anything)
   - $y$ = dependent variable (the output — the quantity being tracked)

2. **Determine the slope ($m$)**:
   - This is the **rate of change**: how much $y$ changes for each 1-unit increase in $x$
   - Often given explicitly in the problem ("grows by 5 per year") or calculated from two data points

3. **Determine the y-intercept ($b$)**:
   - This is the **initial value**: the value of $y$ when $x = 0$
   - Often given explicitly ("started with 200") or calculated from a known data point

4. **Write the model**: $y = mx + b$

5. **Use the model**: Substitute values of $x$ to find $y$, or given $y$ solve for $x$ (this is solving for an input given an output — essentially finding the x-value that produces a particular result).

#### 3.8.2 Interpreting the Slope in Context

The slope always represents the **rate of change of the dependent variable per unit change of the independent variable**, and it always carries **units** (dependent variable units per independent variable unit).

**Example**: A plumber charges a $50 flat fee plus $30 per hour of work. Let $x$ = hours worked and $y$ = total charge in dollars.

$$y = 30x + 50$$

- Slope = $30$ dollars per hour (the plumber's hourly rate)
- Y-intercept = $50$ dollars (the flat fee, charged even for 0 hours)
- If the plumber works 4 hours: $y = 30(4) + 50 = 170$, so the charge is $170

#### 3.8.3 Interpreting the Y-Intercept in Context

The y-intercept represents the **starting value**, **initial amount**, or **fixed cost/base fee** — whatever quantity exists before any units of the independent variable have been applied. In many real-world scenarios, it represents:
- An initial deposit or starting population
- A fixed startup cost or base fee
- A baseline measurement at time zero

**When the y-intercept is zero**: If $b = 0$, the relationship is called a **direct variation** or **proportional relationship**: $y = mx$. The graph passes through the origin.

---

## Part II: Systems of Linear Equations — Where Lines Meet

### 3.9 What Is a System of Linear Equations?

A **system of two linear equations** consists of two linear equations that must be **simultaneously satisfied** — meaning we are looking for a point (or points) that lies on **both** lines at the same time. Geometrically, the solution to a system is the **intersection point** of the two lines.

A system is written as:

$$\begin{cases} a_1x + b_1y = c_1 \\ a_2x + b_2y = c_2 \end{cases}$$

The solution (if it exists) is an ordered pair $(x, y)$ that makes **both** equations true.

---

### 3.10 Types of Solutions

A system of two linear equations in two variables can have exactly **three** possible outcomes in terms of the type of solution set:

#### 3.10.1 One Unique Solution (Consistent and Independent System)

The two lines intersect at exactly one point. This occurs when the lines have **different slopes** ($m_1 \neq m_2$).

$$\text{Example: } y = 2x + 1 \text{ and } y = -x + 4$$

The slopes are $2$ and $-1$, so the lines are not parallel and must intersect somewhere.

#### 3.10.2 No Solution (Inconsistent System)

The two lines are **parallel** and never intersect. This occurs when the lines have **the same slope but different y-intercepts** ($m_1 = m_2$ and $b_1 \neq b_2$).

$$\text{Example: } y = 3x + 2 \text{ and } y = 3x - 5$$

When solved algebraically, you will arrive at a **contradiction** — a statement that is never true, such as $0 = 7$ or $2 = -1$.

#### 3.10.3 Infinitely Many Solutions (Dependent System)

The two lines are **the same line** — they coincide perfectly. This occurs when **both equations are equivalent** (same slope AND same y-intercept, or one equation is a multiple of the other).

$$\text{Example: } y = 2x + 3 \text{ and } 4x - 2y = -6$$

Note: The second equation, rearranged, becomes $2x - y = -3$, or $y = 2x + 3$ — exactly the first equation.

When solved algebraically, you will arrive at an **identity** — a statement that is always true, such as $0 = 0$ or $7 = 7$.

---

### 3.11 Methods for Solving Systems

There are three primary methods for solving systems of linear equations. Each has strengths in particular situations.

#### 3.11.1 The Graphing Method

**Process**: Graph both lines on the same coordinate plane and identify their intersection point.

**Strengths**: Visual, intuitive, good for understanding the geometric meaning.

**Weaknesses**: Imprecise (what if the intersection is at non-integer coordinates?); time-consuming.

**When to use**: When you have a graph already drawn or when both equations are in slope-intercept form and you can quickly sketch them.

**How to do it carefully**:
1. For each equation, find at least two points (using intercepts, slope-intercept, or a table of values).
2. Plot all points on the same coordinate plane.
3. Draw both lines.
4. Identify the point where they cross. Verify the coordinates by substituting into both original equations.

#### 3.11.2 The Substitution Method

**Process**: Solve one equation for one variable, then substitute the result into the other equation.

**Best when**: One of the equations already has a variable isolated (e.g., $y = 3x - 7$), or one variable has a coefficient of $1$ or $-1$ (making it easy to isolate).

**Step-by-step algorithm**:
1. Choose one equation and solve it for one variable (the easiest one to isolate).
2. Substitute the expression you obtained into the **other** equation (not the one you just solved).
3. Solve the resulting equation (which now has only one variable).
4. Substitute your answer back into the expression from Step 1 to find the other variable.
5. Write the solution as an ordered pair $(x, y)$.
6. **Verify**: Substitute both values into both original equations to check.

**Example**:
$$\begin{cases} y = 2x + 1 \quad \text{(Equation 1)} \\ 3x + y = 16 \quad \text{(Equation 2)} \end{cases}$$

Step 1: Equation 1 is already solved for $y$: $y = 2x + 1$

Step 2: Substitute into Equation 2:
$$3x + (2x + 1) = 16$$

Step 3: Solve:
$$5x + 1 = 16 \Rightarrow 5x = 15 \Rightarrow x = 3$$

Step 4: Substitute $x = 3$ into Equation 1:
$$y = 2(3) + 1 = 7$$

Step 5: Solution: $(3, 7)$

Step 6: Verify — Equation 2: $3(3) + 7 = 9 + 7 = 16$ ✓ — Equation 1: $7 = 2(3) + 1 = 7$ ✓

#### 3.11.3 The Elimination Method (Addition/Subtraction Method)

**Process**: Multiply one or both equations by constants so that adding or subtracting the equations eliminates one variable.

**Best when**: Both equations are in standard form $Ax + By = C$, and the coefficients are such that elimination is straightforward (either the coefficients of one variable are already the same, or they are the same except for the sign, or they can be easily made the same by multiplying).

**Step-by-step algorithm**:
1. Align both equations in standard form.
2. Choose which variable to eliminate ($x$ or $y$).
3. If needed, multiply one or both equations by appropriate constants so that the coefficients of the chosen variable are **equal in magnitude but opposite in sign** (or equal the same sign, in which case you subtract instead of add).
4. Add (or subtract) the two equations. This will eliminate the chosen variable, leaving a one-variable equation.
5. Solve for the remaining variable.
6. Substitute this value back into either original equation to find the other variable.
7. Write the solution as an ordered pair $(x, y)$.
8. Verify by substituting into both original equations.

**Example 1 (Simple elimination)**:
$$\begin{cases} 2x + 3y = 12 \\ 2x - y = 4 \end{cases}$$

The $x$ coefficients are already the same. Subtract the second equation from the first:
$$(2x + 3y) - (2x - y) = 12 - 4$$
$$4y = 8 \Rightarrow y = 2$$

Substitute $y = 2$ into Equation 2: $2x - 2 = 4 \Rightarrow 2x = 6 \Rightarrow x = 3$

Solution: $(3, 2)$

**Example 2 (Scaling needed)**:
$$\begin{cases} 3x + 2y = 11 \\ 2x + 5y = 3 \end{cases}$$

To eliminate $x$, multiply the first equation by 2 and the second by 3:
$$6x + 4y = 22$$
$$6x + 15y = 9$$

Subtract: $(6x + 4y) - (6x + 15y) = 22 - 9 \Rightarrow -11y = 13 \Rightarrow y = -\frac{13}{11}$

Then back-substitute to find $x$. (The answer might be a fraction — that's perfectly fine and expected in some problems.)

**Special consideration**: If elimination produces $0 = 0$, the system has infinitely many solutions (dependent). If elimination produces a contradiction like $0 = 5$, the system has no solution (inconsistent).

---

### 3.12 Systems of Equations in Word Problems

Word problems involving systems are among the most challenging problems in algebra because they require translating natural language into mathematical equations. Here are the most common types:

#### 3.12.1 Mixture/Collection Problems

These involve combining two (or more) types of items with different values to achieve a mixture with a specific total value.

**Setup**: $(\text{value}_1)(\text{amount}_1) + (\text{value}_2)(\text{amount}_2) = (\text{value}_3)(\text{total amount})$

**Example**: A coffee shop sells premium beans at $8/lb and standard beans at $5/lb. How much of each should be mixed to make 20 lbs of blend worth $6.50/lb?

Let $x$ = lbs of premium, $y$ = lbs of standard.

Equation 1 (total weight): $x + y = 20$
Equation 2 (total value): $8x + 5y = 6.50(20) = 130$

Solve: $y = 20 - x$; $8x + 5(20 - x) = 130$; $8x + 100 - 5x = 130$; $3x = 30$; $x = 10$, $y = 10$.

#### 3.12.2 Current/Speed Problems

**Still water / no wind formula**: $\text{Speed with current} = r + c$; $\text{Speed against current} = r - c$ (where $r$ = speed in still water and $c$ = current speed).

#### 3.12.3 Investment Problems

Different amounts are invested at different interest rates, and the total interest earned is known.

**Setup**: $r_1 x + r_2 y = \text{total interest}$, where $x + y = \text{total investment}$.

#### 3.12.4 General Strategy for Word Problems

1. **Read the entire problem carefully** — at least twice.
2. **Define your variables** explicitly (let $x$ = ... and $y$ = ...).
3. **Identify two relationships** in the problem — these become your two equations.
4. **Solve the system** using your preferred method.
5. **Check both solutions** in the context of the original word problem (make sure the answer makes real-world sense — no negative distances, fractional people, etc.).
6. **Answer the question asked** — sometimes the solution $(x, y)$ is the answer, but sometimes the question asks for a derived quantity.

---

## Part III: Quadratic Functions — The Algebra of Parabolas

### 3.13 What Is a Quadratic Function?

A **quadratic function** is any function that can be written in the form:

$$f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$$

where $a \neq 0$ (if $a = 0$, it degenerates to a linear function). The graph of any quadratic function is a **parabola** — a symmetric, U-shaped (or inverted U-shaped) curve.

The quadratic function is the next level of complexity beyond linear functions. Where linear functions have a constant rate of change, quadratic functions have a **changing rate of change** — the slope itself varies from point to point. This produces the characteristic curved shape.

---

### 3.14 Standard Form: $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$

This is the most common form and is the one a quadratic is usually presented in initially.

#### 3.14.1 Properties Readable from Standard Form

- **$c$ is the y-intercept**: The graph intersects the y-axis at $(0, c)$. This is because $f(0) = a(0)^2 + b(0) + c = c$.
- **$a$ determines direction of opening**:
  - If $a > 0$: The parabola opens **upward** (shaped like a smile or the letter U) — the vertex is the **minimum** point (the lowest value of the function).
  - If $a < 0$: The parabola opens **downward** (shaped like an upside-down U or a frown) — the vertex is the **maximum** point (the highest value of the function).
- **$|a|$ determines "width"**:
  - Larger $|a|$ → narrower (steeper, more compressed) parabola
  - Smaller $|a|$ → wider (flatter, more spread-out) parabola
  - For comparison: $y = 5x^2$ is narrower than $y = x^2$, and $y = \frac{1}{3}x^2$ is wider than $y = x^2$.
- **The vertex x-coordinate**: $h = -\frac{b}{2a}$

This is one of the most important formulas in all of high school algebra. The x-coordinate of the vertex can be found using this formula, derived from completing the square or from the fact that the vertex lies on the axis of symmetry.

To find the y-coordinate of the vertex, substitute $h = -\frac{b}{2a}$ back into the function: $k = f\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)$.

**Vertex**: $\left(-\frac{b}{2a},\ f\left(-\frac{b}{2a}\right)\right)$

---

### 3.15 Vertex Form: $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$

This form makes the vertex immediately visible:

- **Vertex**: $(h, k)$
- **Axis of symmetry**: $x = h$

Note the sign structure: it is $(x - h)^2$. If the equation is $y = 2(x - 3)^2 + 5$, then $h = 3$ and $k = 5$, so the vertex is $(3, 5)$. If the equation is $y = 2(x + 4)^2 - 7$, then $h = -4$ (because $x + 4 = x - (-4)$) and $k = -7$, so the vertex is $(-4, -7)$.

The vertex form also reveals the **transformations** applied to the parent function $y = x^2$:
- $a$: vertical stretch/compression and reflection
- $h$: horizontal shift (right if positive, left if negative)
- $k$: vertical shift (up if positive, down if negative)

---

### 3.16 Intercept (Factored) Form: $f(x) = a(x - p)(x - q)$

This form makes the x-intercepts immediately visible:

- **X-intercepts**: $(p, 0)$ and $(q, 0)$
- These are also called **roots** or **zeros** of the quadratic
- **Axis of symmetry**: $x = \frac{p + q}{2}$ (the midpoint of the two roots)
- **Vertex x-coordinate**: $h = \frac{p + q}{2}$
- **Vertex y-coordinate**: $k = f\left(\frac{p + q}{2}\right)$

This form is derived from the factored representation using the Zero Product Property. It also beautifully illustrates why the vertex always lies exactly halfway between the two roots (when the quadratic has two real roots).

---

### 3.17 Converting Between Forms

Being able to convert fluently between all three forms is a critical skill:

#### 3.17.1 Standard Form → Vertex Form (Completing the Square)

**Process**:
1. Start with $ax^2 + bx + c$
2. Factor $a$ from the $x^2$ and $x$ terms: $a\left(x^2 + \frac{b}{a}x\right) + c$
3. Half the $x$-coefficient inside: $\frac{b}{2a}$, then square it: $\left(\frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 = \frac{b^2}{4a^2}$
4. Add and subtract this value inside the parentheses
5. Perfect square trinomial forms: $a\left(\left(x + \frac{b}{2a}\right)^2 - \frac{b^2}{4a^2}\right) + c$
6. Distribute $a$ and simplify the constants

**Example**: Convert $f(x) = 2x^2 + 12x + 11$ to vertex form.

$2(x^2 + 6x) + 11$

Half of 6 is 3; $3^2 = 9$:

$2(x^2 + 6x + 9 - 9) + 11$

$2((x + 3)^2 - 9) + 11$

$2(x + 3)^2 - 18 + 11$

$2(x + 3)^2 - 7$

Vertex: $(-3, -7)$

#### 3.17.2 Intercept Form → Standard Form (FOIL/Expand)

Multiply the binomials using FOIL (First, Outer, Inner, Last), then distribute $a$:

**Example**: $f(x) = 3(x - 1)(x + 5)$

$= 3(x^2 + 5x - x - 5)$

$= 3(x^2 + 4x - 5)$

$= 3x^2 + 12x - 15$

#### 3.17.3 Standard Form → Intercept Form (Factoring)

Factor the quadratic expression. This requires finding two numbers that multiply to give the constant term (or $ac$ when $a \neq 1$) and add to give the linear coefficient. See the Factoring section below.

---

### 3.18 The Quadratic Formula

The **quadratic formula** provides a universal method for finding the roots (x-intercepts) of any quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$:

$$x = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}$$

This formula is derived by completing the square on the general quadratic $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$. It works for **every** quadratic equation, regardless of whether the quadratic can be factored over the integers.

**How to use it**:
1. Ensure the equation is set equal to zero: $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$
2. Identify $a$, $b$, and $c$ (including their signs!)
3. Substitute into the formula
4. Simplify the expression under the square root (the discriminant)
5. Split into two solutions: one with $+$ and one with $-$

**Example**: Solve $2x^2 - 5x - 3 = 0$

$a = 2$, $b = -5$, $c = -3$

$$x = \frac{-(-5) \pm \sqrt{(-5)^2 - 4(2)(-3)}}{2(2)} = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{25 + 24}}{4} = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{49}}{4} = \frac{5 \pm 7}{4}$$

$$x = \frac{5 + 7}{4} = \frac{12}{4} = 3 \quad \text{or} \quad x = \frac{5 - 7}{4} = \frac{-2}{4} = -\frac{1}{2}$$

---

### 3.19 The Discriminant

The expression under the square root in the quadratic formula, $b^2 - 4ac$, is called the **discriminant**. It tells you the **nature of the solutions** without actually solving the equation:

| Discriminant Value | Nature of Solutions | Graph Behavior |
|---|---|---|
| $b^2 - 4ac > 0$ | **Two distinct real solutions** (two real roots) | Parabola crosses the x-axis at **two** points |
| $b^2 - 4ac = 0$ | **One real solution** (repeated root; also called a "double root") | Parabola touches the x-axis at exactly **one** point (the vertex lies on the x-axis) |
| $b^2 - 4ac < 0$ | **No real solutions** (two complex/imaginary roots) | Parabola does **not** intersect the x-axis at all |

**When the discriminant is a perfect square** (and $a$, $b$, $c$ are rational), the quadratic can be factored over the integers. When it is positive but not a perfect square, the roots are irrational (they involve square roots).

**Example analysis**:
- $x^2 - 6x + 9 = 0$: Discriminant = $36 - 36 = 0$ → one real (double) root. Indeed, $(x-3)^2 = 0$ gives $x = 3$ (repeated).
- $x^2 - 4x + 5 = 0$: Discriminant = $16 - 20 = -4$ → no real roots. The parabola $y = x^2 - 4x + 5$ never touches the x-axis.
- $x^2 - x - 6 = 0$: Discriminant = $1 + 24 = 25$ → two real roots. Indeed, $(x-3)(x+2) = 0$ gives $x = 3$ and $x = -2$.

---

### 3.20 Properties of Quadratic Functions — A Comprehensive Summary

#### 3.20.1 The Vertex

The **vertex** is the highest or lowest point on the parabola. It represents the **maximum value** (if $a < 0$) or **minimum value** (if $a > 0$) of the function.

- **x-coordinate**: $h = -\frac{b}{2a}$ (from standard form) or $h$ (from vertex form) or $h = \frac{p+q}{2}$ (from intercept form)
- **y-coordinate**: $k = f(h)$

#### 3.20.2 The Axis of Symmetry

The **axis of symmetry** is the vertical line $x = h$ that divides the parabola into two mirror-image halves. Every point on one side of the axis has a corresponding point on the other side at the same height.

#### 3.20.3 The Domain and Range

- **Domain**: All real numbers, $(-\infty, \infty)$ — the parabola extends infinitely in both horizontal directions.
- **Range**:
  - If $a > 0$: $[k, \infty)$ — the function takes on all values from the minimum $k$ upward
  - If $a < 0$: $(-\infty, k]$ — the function takes on all values from the maximum $k$ downward

#### 3.20.4 Symmetry and Reflections

The parabola is symmetric about its axis of symmetry. If $(h + d, y_0)$ is on the graph, then $(h - d, y_0)$ is also on the graph. This means the function values at equal distances to the left and right of the vertex are the same: $f(h + d) = f(h - d)$.

#### 3.20.5 Intervals of Increase/Decrease

- If $a > 0$: The function **decreases** on $(-\infty, h]$ and **increases** on $[h, \infty)$.
- If $a < 0$: The function **increases** on $(-\infty, h]$ and **decreases** on $[h, \infty)$.

The vertex is the turning point where the function changes from increasing to decreasing (or vice versa).

---

### 3.21 Factoring Quadratics

Factoring is the process of expressing a quadratic as a product of two binomials. It is the bridge between standard form and intercept form, and it provides the roots directly.

#### 3.21.1 When $a = 1$: $x^2 + bx + c$

Find two numbers $p$ and $q$ such that:
- $p + q = b$ (they add to the middle coefficient)
- $pq = c$ (they multiply to the constant term)

Then: $x^2 + bx + c = (x + p)(x + q)$

**Example**: Factor $x^2 + 7x + 12$

We need two numbers that add to 7 and multiply to 12. Those numbers are 3 and 4.

$x^2 + 7x + 12 = (x + 3)(x + 4)$

Roots: $x = -3$ and $x = -4$

**Example with negatives**: Factor $x^2 - 5x + 6$

We need two numbers that add to $-5$ and multiply to $6$. Those numbers are $-2$ and $-3$.

$x^2 - 5x + 6 = (x - 2)(x - 3)$

Roots: $x = 2$ and $x = 3$

**Example with mixed signs**: Factor $x^2 + x - 12$

We need two numbers that add to $1$ and multiply to $-12$. Those numbers are $4$ and $-3$.

$x^2 + x - 12 = (x + 4)(x - 3)$

Roots: $x = -4$ and $x = 3$

#### 3.21.2 When $a \neq 1$: The AC Method

For $ax^2 + bx + c$ where $a \neq 1$:

1. Multiply $a \cdot c$ (the leading coefficient times the constant term).
2. Find two numbers $m$ and $n$ such that $m \cdot n = ac$ and $m + n = b$.
3. Rewrite the middle term $bx$ as $mx + nx$.
4. Factor by grouping: group the first two terms and the last two terms, factor out the GCF from each group, then factor out the common binomial.

**Example**: Factor $6x^2 + 11x - 10$

1. $a \cdot c = 6 \cdot (-10) = -60$
2. Find two numbers that multiply to $-60$ and add to $11$. Those numbers are $15$ and $-4$ (since $15 \cdot (-4) = -60$ and $15 + (-4) = 11$).
3. Rewrite: $6x^2 + 15x - 4x - 10$
4. Group: $(6x^2 + 15x) + (-4x - 10)$
5. Factor GCF from each: $3x(2x + 5) - 2(2x + 5)$
6. Factor common binomial: $(2x + 5)(3x - 2)$

Roots: $x = -\frac{5}{2}$ and $x = \frac{2}{3}$

#### 3.21.3 Special Factoring Patterns

Always check for these before applying other methods:

**Difference of Squares**: $a^2 - b^2 = (a + b)(a - b)$

**Example**: $x^2 - 25 = (x + 5)(x - 5)$

**Example**: $4x^2 - 9 = (2x + 3)(2x - 3)$

**Perfect Square Trinomials**:
- $a^2 + 2ab + b^2 = (a + b)^2$
- $a^2 - 2ab + b^2 = (a - b)^2$

**Example**: $x^2 + 10x + 25 = (x + 5)^2$

**Example**: $9x^2 - 30x + 25 = (3x - 5)^2$

**Sum/Difference of Cubes** (relevant when the quadratic involves cubic terms):
- $a^3 + b^3 = (a + b)(a^2 - ab + b^2)$
- $a^3 - b^3 = (a - b)(a^2 + ab + b^2)$

#### 3.21.4 The Zero Product Property

Once a quadratic is factored, the **Zero Product Property** states that if the product of two expressions equals zero, then at least one of the expressions must equal zero:

$$\text{If } (x - p)(x - q) = 0, \text{ then } x - p = 0 \text{ or } x - q = 0$$

Therefore: $x = p$ or $x = q$.

This is the fundamental principle that connects factoring to finding roots.

---

### 3.22 Graphing Quadratic Functions — A Complete Strategy

To graph a quadratic function $f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c$ by hand:

1. **Determine the direction of opening**: $a > 0$ → opens up; $a < 0$ → opens down.
2. **Find the vertex**: $h = -\frac{b}{2a}$, $k = f(h)$. Plot the vertex.
3. **Find the y-intercept**: $(0, c)$. Plot it.
4. **Find the x-intercepts** (if they exist): Set $f(x) = 0$ and solve (by factoring or quadratic formula). Plot them.
5. **Use symmetry**: The y-intercept $(0, c)$ has a mirror image across the axis of symmetry at $(2h, c)$. Plot this point.
6. **Plot additional points** if needed by substituting $x$-values near the vertex.
7. **Draw a smooth curve** through all points (not straight line segments!) — the curve should be symmetric about the axis of symmetry.

**Key graphing principle**: Parabolas are smooth, continuous curves. They do not have corners, cusps, or sharp turns. The vertex is the single point where the curve changes direction.

---

### 3.23 Sum and Product of Roots

For the quadratic equation $ax^2 + bx + c = 0$ with roots $r_1$ and $r_2$:

- **Sum of roots**: $r_1 + r_2 = -\frac{b}{a}$
- **Product of roots**: $r_1 \cdot r_2 = \frac{c}{a}$

These relationships (known as **Vieta's formulas**) are useful for:
- Checking your work after factoring
- Finding the sum or product of roots without actually solving
- Constructing a quadratic equation given its roots
- Determining if roots are positive, negative, or mixed based on the signs of $a$, $b$, and $c$

**Example**: For $3x^2 - 7x + 2 = 0$:
- Sum of roots = $-\frac{-7}{3} = \frac{7}{3}$
- Product of roots = $\frac{2}{3}$
- Indeed, factoring gives $(3x - 1)(x - 2) = 0$, so roots are $\frac{1}{3}$ and $2$. Sum: $\frac{1}{3} + 2 = \frac{7}{3}$ ✓. Product: $\frac{1}{3} \cdot 2 = \frac{2}{3}$ ✓.

---

### 3.24 Quadratic Transformations

The vertex form $f(x) = a(x - h)^2 + k$ reveals how the graph of the parent function $f(x) = x^2$ is transformed:

| Parameter | Effect on Graph |
|---|---|
| $a$ (where $|a| > 1$) | Vertical stretch (narrower parabola) |
| $a$ (where $0 < |a| < 1$) | Vertical compression (wider parabola) |
| $a$ (where $a < 0$) | Reflection across the x-axis (opens downward) |
| $h$ (where $h > 0$) | Horizontal shift right by $h$ units |
| $h$ (where $h < 0$) | Horizontal shift left by $|h|$ units |
| $k$ (where $k > 0$) | Vertical shift up by $k$ units |
| $k$ (where $k < 0$) | Vertical shift down by $|k|$ units |

**Order of transformations** (applied to $x^2$): Horizontal shift → Stretch/Compress/Reflect → Vertical shift.

---

## Part IV: Algebraic Reasoning — Rates, Ratios, Proportions, and Advanced Concepts

### 3.25 Ratios and Proportions

#### 3.25.1 Ratios

A **ratio** is a comparison of two quantities by division. It can be expressed in several forms:
- $a : b$ (read as "$a$ to $b$")
- $\frac{a}{b}$
- "$a$ to $b$"

**Key properties of ratios**:
- Ratios are **always simplified** (reduced to lowest terms)
- Ratios compare parts to parts or parts to whole
- Ratios do **not** give exact values — they give relative proportions
- If a ratio is $3:5$, the actual quantities could be 3 and 5, or 6 and 10, or 30 and 50, etc. — any pair in the same proportion

**Part-to-part vs. part-to-whole**: If a class has 12 boys and 18 girls:
- Ratio of boys to girls = $12:18 = 2:3$ (part-to-part)
- Ratio of boys to total students = $12:30 = 2:5$ (part-to-whole)

#### 3.25.2 Proportions

A **proportion** is an equation stating that two ratios are equal:

$$\frac{a}{b} = \frac{c}{d}$$

**Solving proportions**: Cross-multiply:

$$ad = bc$$

This is valid because multiplying both sides by the common denominator $bd$ gives $ad = bc$.

**Example**: If $\frac{x}{15} = \frac{8}{20}$, then $20x = 15 \cdot 8 = 120$, so $x = 6$.

#### 3.25.3 Scale Factor

In **similar figures** (figures with the same shape but different sizes), the **scale factor** is the ratio of any pair of corresponding side lengths.

- If the scale factor from Figure A to Figure B is $k$, then every length in B is $k$ times the corresponding length in A.
- **Perimeter ratio** = scale factor = $k$
- **Area ratio** = $k^2$ (the square of the scale factor)
- **Volume ratio** = $k^3$ (the cube of the scale factor, for 3D figures)

**Example**: Two similar rectangles have a scale factor of $3:2$ from the smaller to the larger. If the smaller rectangle has area $20$ square units, the larger has area:

$$\text{Area ratio} = \left(\frac{3}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{9}{4}$$

$$\text{Larger area} = 20 \cdot \frac{9}{4} = 45 \text{ square units}$$

---

### 3.26 Rates

A **rate** is a special type of ratio that compares two quantities with **different units**. It defines the amount of one quantity **per unit** of another.

**Common rates**: speed (miles per hour), density (grams per cubic centimeter), unit price (dollars per pound), heart rate (beats per minute), flow rate (liters per minute).

#### 3.26.1 Average Speed

**Critical formula**:

$$\text{Average speed} = \frac{\text{Total distance}}{\text{Total time}}$$

**This is NOT the average of the speeds!** This is one of the most commonly misunderstood concepts in all of mathematics.

**Example**: A car travels 120 miles at 40 mph and then 120 miles at 60 mph. What is the average speed for the entire trip?

- Time for first part: $\frac{120}{40} = 3$ hours
- Time for second part: $\frac{120}{60} = 2$ hours
- Total distance: $120 + 120 = 240$ miles
- Total time: $3 + 2 = 5$ hours
- Average speed: $\frac{240}{5} = 48$ mph

Note: The average of 40 and 60 is 50, but the average speed is **not** 50 mph. The car spent more time traveling at the slower speed (3 hours at 40 mph vs. 2 hours at 60 mph), so the average is weighted toward 40 mph.

#### 3.26.2 The Distance-Speed-Time Relationship

$$\text{Distance} = \text{Speed} \times \text{Time}$$

This can be rearranged:
- $\text{Speed} = \frac{\text{Distance}}{\text{Time}}$
- $\text{Time} = \frac{\text{Distance}}{\text{Speed}}$

**Mnemonic**: Draw a triangle with $D$ on top, $S$ and $T$ on the bottom. Cover the variable you want to find, and the remaining two show you the formula.

---

### 3.27 Percents

#### 3.27.1 The Percent Formula

$$\text{Percent} = \frac{\text{Part}}{\text{Whole}} \times 100$$

This can be rearranged:
- $\text{Part} = \text{Percent} \times \text{Whole}$ (where percent is in decimal form)
- $\text{Whole} = \frac{\text{Part}}{\text{Percent}}$

**Two versions of percent problems**:
- **"Is" version**: $\frac{\text{part}}{\text{whole}} \times 100 = \text{percent}$ → "What percent of 80 is 20?" → $\frac{20}{80} \times 100 = 25\%$
- **"Of" version**: $\text{percent} \times \text{whole} = \text{part}$ → "What is 25% of 80?" → $0.25 \times 80 = 20$

#### 3.27.2 Percent Change

$$\text{Percent Change} = \frac{\text{New Value} - \text{Old Value}}{\text{Old Value}} \times 100\%$$

- Positive result → percent increase
- Negative result → percent decrease
- The denominator is **always the original (old) value**, not the new value

**Example**: A stock went from $50 to $65. Percent change = $\frac{65 - 50}{50} \times 100\% = \frac{15}{50} \times 100\% = 30\%$ increase.

#### 3.27.3 Percent Increase and Decrease — Multiplicative Approach

To apply a percent increase or decrease, convert the percent to a decimal and multiply:

- **Percent increase of $p\%$**: multiply by $(1 + \frac{p}{100})$
- **Percent decrease of $p\%$**: multiply by $(1 - \frac{p}{100})$

**Example**: A $400 jacket is on sale for 30% off. Sale price = $400 \times (1 - 0.30) = 400 \times 0.70 = \$280$.

**Example**: A population of 10,000 grows by 5% per year. After one year: $10{,}000 \times 1.05 = 10{,}500$.

#### 3.27.4 Successive Percent Changes

When multiple percent changes are applied in sequence, they are applied **multiplicatively**, not additively. You multiply the factors, not add the percentages.

**Example**: A price of $200 is increased by 10% and then decreased by 10%.

- After increase: $200 \times 1.10 = 220$
- After decrease: $220 \times 0.90 = 198$

The final price is $198, not $200. A 10% increase followed by a 10% decrease does **not** return you to the original value. This is because the 10% decrease is applied to a larger amount ($220), so the absolute decrease ($22) is larger than the absolute increase ($20).

**General formula for successive changes**: If a quantity is changed by $p\%$ and then by $q\%$, the overall factor is $(1 + \frac{p}{100})(1 + \frac{q}{100})$.

---

### 3.28 Factor of Change

The **factor of change** is a powerful concept for understanding how changes in one quantity affect related quantities through a formula.

#### 3.28.1 The Method

1. Start with the formula relating the quantities.
2. Eliminate all constants (they don't change).
3. Replace each variable with its factor of change.
4. Simplify to find the factor of change of the dependent quantity.

#### 3.28.2 Examples

**Square area**: $A = s^2$. If the side length increases by 10%, the new side is $1.10s$.

New area = $(1.10s)^2 = 1.21s^2 = 1.21A$.

The area increases by **21%** (not 20%!). This is because the factor is squared.

**Cone volume**: $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$. If the radius decreases by 20% and the height increases by 10%:

New $r$ factor = $0.80$; new $h$ factor = $1.10$.

New volume factor = $(0.80)^2 \cdot (1.10) = 0.64 \cdot 1.10 = 0.704$.

The new volume is 70.4% of the original, meaning a **29.6% decrease**.

**Electric power**: $P = \frac{V^2}{R}$. If voltage is halved ($V$ factor = $0.50$):

New power factor = $(0.50)^2 = 0.25$.

Power drops to 25% of original — a **75% decrease**.

**Triangle area and scale factor**: If the area of a triangle decreases to 64% of the original:

$A_{\text{new}} = s^2 \cdot A_{\text{old}} = 0.64 \cdot A_{\text{old}}$

$s^2 = 0.64$

$s = \sqrt{0.64} = 0.80$

The side length decreased to 80% of original, meaning a **20% decrease** in side length.

---

### 3.29 Mixture Problems

Mixture problems involve combining substances with different concentrations or values to create a mixture with a target concentration or value.

**The fundamental equation**:

$$(\%_1)(\text{amount}_1) + (\%_2)(\text{amount}_2) = (\%_3)(\text{total amount})$$

This equation states that the total amount of "pure" substance in the mixture equals the sum of the "pure" substances from each component.

**Example**: A chemist has a 20% acid solution and a 50% acid solution. How many liters of each should be mixed to get 10 liters of a 35% acid solution?

Let $x$ = liters of 20% solution, $y$ = liters of 50% solution.

Equation 1 (total volume): $x + y = 10$
Equation 2 (total acid): $0.20x + 0.50y = 0.35(10) = 3.5$

From Equation 1: $y = 10 - x$
Substitute: $0.20x + 0.50(10 - x) = 3.5$
$0.20x + 5 - 0.50x = 3.5$
$-0.30x = -1.5$
$x = 5$, $y = 5$

Answer: 5 liters of each solution.

---

### 3.30 Functions — Advanced Concepts

#### 3.30.1 Function Notation

The notation $f(x)$ does **not** mean "$f$ times $x$." It means "the output of the function $f$ when the input is $x$." It is read as "$f$ of $x$."

**Evaluating functions**: To find $f(3)$, substitute $3$ for every instance of $x$ in the function's expression.

**Example**: If $f(x) = 2x^2 - 3x + 1$, then:
- $f(3) = 2(9) - 9 + 1 = 18 - 9 + 1 = 10$
- $f(-2) = 2(4) - 3(-2) + 1 = 8 + 6 + 1 = 15$
- $f(a) = 2a^2 - 3a + 1$ (substitute the variable $a$ for $x$)

#### 3.30.2 Composite Functions

The composition $f(g(x))$, read as "$f$ of $g$ of $x$," means: first apply $g$ to $x$, then apply $f$ to the result.

$$f(g(x)) = f(\text{output of } g \text{ at } x)$$

**Order matters**: In general, $f(g(x)) \neq g(f(x))$. You must apply the inner function first, then the outer function.

**Example**: If $f(x) = x^2$ and $g(x) = x + 3$:
- $f(g(x)) = f(x + 3) = (x + 3)^2 = x^2 + 6x + 9$
- $g(f(x)) = g(x^2) = x^2 + 3$

Clearly, $x^2 + 6x + 9 \neq x^2 + 3$ in general.

#### 3.30.3 "Wacky Functions"

Sometimes a test will define a new, unfamiliar symbol and ask you to use it. These are sometimes called "wacky functions" or "defined operations." The key is to follow the given definition exactly.

**Example**: Define $a \star b = a^2 + 2ab - b^2$. Find $3 \star 2$.

$3 \star 2 = 3^2 + 2(3)(2) - 2^2 = 9 + 12 - 4 = 17$

The symbol $\star$ has no inherent mathematical meaning — it is defined purely by the problem. Your job is to substitute the given values into the definition and compute.

#### 3.30.4 "As a Function Of"

When a problem asks you to express something "as a function of" a variable, that variable is the **independent variable** (the input, $x$). The phrase "the term before 'of' is $x$; the term after 'of' is $y$" is a useful mnemonic.

**Example**: "Express the area of a square as a function of its side length" → $A(s) = s^2$

**Example**: "Express the volume of a cone as a function of its radius (with fixed height $h$)" → $V(r) = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$

---

### 3.31 Number Properties

#### 3.31.1 Consecutive Integers

- **Consecutive integers**: $n, n+1, n+2, n+3, \ldots$
- **Consecutive even integers**: $n, n+2, n+4, n+6, \ldots$ (where $n$ is even)
- **Consecutive odd integers**: $n, n+2, n+4, n+6, \ldots$ (where $n$ is odd)

Note: Consecutive even or odd integers differ by 2, not by 1.

#### 3.31.2 GCF and LCM

- **Greatest Common Factor (GCF)**: The largest positive integer that divides evenly into all given numbers.
  - **How to find**: Prime factor each number. For each prime that appears in all factorizations, take the lowest power that appears. Multiply these together.
  - **Example**: GCF of 24 and 36. $24 = 2^3 \cdot 3^1$, $36 = 2^2 \cdot 3^2$. Lowest powers: $2^2$ and $3^1$. GCF = $4 \cdot 3 = 12$.

- **Least Common Multiple (LCM)**: The smallest positive integer that all given numbers divide into evenly.
  - **How to find**: Prime factor each number. For each prime that appears in any factorization, take the **highest** power that appears. Multiply these together.
  - **Example**: LCM of 24 and 36. Highest powers: $2^3$ and $3^2$. LCM = $8 \cdot 9 = 72$.

**Useful relationship**: $\text{GCF}(a, b) \times \text{LCM}(a, b) = a \times b$

#### 3.31.3 Rational and Irrational Numbers

- **Rational numbers**: Can be expressed as $\frac{p}{q}$ where $p$ and $q$ are integers and $q \neq 0$. Their decimal representations either **terminate** or **repeat** a pattern.
  - Examples: $\frac{1}{2} = 0.5$, $\frac{1}{3} = 0.333\ldots$, $-7$, $0$, $\frac{22}{7}$

- **Irrational numbers**: Cannot be expressed as a fraction of integers. Their decimal representations **never terminate and never repeat**.
  - Examples: $\sqrt{2}$, $\sqrt{3}$, $\pi$, $e$

**Key closure properties**:
- Rational + Rational = Rational
- Rational × Rational = Rational
- Irrational + Rational = Irrational
- Irrational × Rational (non-zero) = Irrational
- Irrational × Irrational = Can be either (e.g., $\sqrt{2} \cdot \sqrt{2} = 2$, which is rational)

#### 3.31.4 Absolute Value

The absolute value of a number is its distance from zero on the number line, regardless of direction:

$$|x| = \begin{cases} x & \text{if } x \geq 0 \\ -x & \text{if } x < 0 \end{cases}$$

**Properties**:
- $|x| \geq 0$ always (absolute value is never negative)
- $|x| = |-x|$
- $|xy| = |x| \cdot |y|$
- $|x + y| \leq |x| + |y|$ (triangle inequality)

**Solving absolute value equations**: Consider both cases.
- $|x| = 5$ → $x = 5$ or $x = -5$
- $|2x - 3| = 7$ → $2x - 3 = 7$ or $2x - 3 = -7$ → $x = 5$ or $x = -2$

**Absolute value inequalities**:
- $|x| < a$ → $-a < x < a$ (AND statement)
- $|x| > a$ → $x < -a$ or $x > a$ (OR statement)

#### 3.31.5 Squaring and the Order of Operations

In the expression $a^2$, only $a$ is squared. But in $(2a)^2$, the entire quantity $2a$ is squared:

- $3^2 = 9$
- $(-3)^2 = (-3)(-3) = 9$
- $-3^2 = -(3^2) = -9$ (the negative sign is applied AFTER squaring)
- $(2 \cdot 3)^2 = 6^2 = 36$
- $2 \cdot 3^2 = 2 \cdot 9 = 18$

**Critical distinction**: $-a^2$ means $-(a^2)$, not $(-a)^2$. The exponent applies before the negation unless parentheses indicate otherwise.

---

### 3.32 Probability

#### 3.32.1 Basic Probability

The probability of an event $E$ is:

$$P(E) = \frac{\text{Number of favorable outcomes}}{\text{Total number of possible outcomes}}$$

**Key properties**:
- $0 \leq P(E) \leq 1$ for any event
- $P(E) = 0$ means the event is impossible
- $P(E) = 1$ means the event is certain
- $P(\text{not } E) = 1 - P(E)$ (the complement rule)

#### 3.32.2 Permutations

A **permutation** is an arrangement of objects in a specific order. The number of ways to arrange $n$ distinct objects is $n!$ (n factorial):

$$n! = n \times (n-1) \times (n-2) \times \cdots \times 2 \times 1$$

By definition, $0! = 1$.

**Example**: $5! = 5 \times 4 \times 3 \times 2 \times 1 = 120$

**Permutations of $r$ objects chosen from $n$ objects** (where order matters):

$$_nP_r = \frac{n!}{(n-r)!}$$

---

### 3.33 Statistics — Measures of Center and Spread

#### 3.33.1 Measures of Center

**Mean (Arithmetic Average)**:

$$\bar{x} = \frac{\sum x_i}{n} = \frac{x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_n}{n}$$

- The mean is the "balancing point" of the data
- The mean is **sensitive to outliers** — a single extreme value can pull the mean significantly in its direction

**Weighted Average**:

$$\bar{x} = \frac{n_1 \bar{x}_1 + n_2 \bar{x}_2 + \cdots + n_k \bar{x}_k}{n_1 + n_2 + \cdots + n_k}$$

This is used when different groups have different sizes and different means.

**Median**: The middle value when data is arranged in order. If there is an even number of data points, the median is the average of the two middle values.

- The median is **resistant to outliers** — extreme values do not affect it

**Mode**: The most frequently occurring value in the data set.

- A data set can have one mode, more than one mode, or no mode at all

#### 3.33.2 Measures of Spread

**Range**: $\text{Range} = \text{Maximum} - \text{Minimum}$

**Interquartile Range (IQR)**: $\text{IQR} = Q_3 - Q_1$ (the middle 50% of data)

**Standard Deviation ($\sigma$)**: A measure of how spread out the data is from the mean.
- Data spread out → larger $\sigma$
- Data grouped together → smaller $\sigma$
- $\sigma = 0$ only when all data values are identical

#### 3.33.3 Shape of Distribution and the Relationship Between Mean and Median

| Shape | Mean vs. Median | Explanation |
|---|---|---|
| **Symmetric** | Mean ≈ Median | Data is balanced on both sides |
| **Skewed Right** (tail to the right) | Mean > Median | Outliers on the high end pull the mean up |
| **Skewed Left** (tail to the left) | Mean < Median | Outliers on the low end pull the mean down |

This relationship is a powerful diagnostic tool: if you know the shape of a distribution, you can predict whether the mean or median will be larger, and vice versa.

#### 3.33.4 Types of Statistical Studies

- **Population ($N$)**: The complete set of individuals you wish to study. Often impractical to measure entirely.
- **Sample**: A subset of the population. Must be selected **randomly** to avoid bias.
- **Observational Study**: Researchers observe without intervening. Can identify **associations** but **not causation**.
- **Experiment**: Researchers assign treatments to subjects randomly. Allows inference of **cause and effect**.

---

### 3.34 Scientific Notation

Scientific notation expresses numbers in the form:

$$a \times 10^n$$

where $1 \leq |a| < 10$ and $n$ is an integer.

**Converting to scientific notation**:
- Move the decimal point so that exactly one non-zero digit is to its left.
- Count the number of places you moved the decimal. This count is the exponent $n$.
- If you moved the decimal to the left, $n$ is positive. If you moved it to the right, $n$ is negative.

**Examples**:
- $4{,}300 = 4.3 \times 10^3$
- $0.00072 = 7.2 \times 10^{-4}$
