- $50 = 5.0 \times 10^1$

**Arithmetic in scientific notation**:
- **Multiplication**: Multiply the coefficients, add the exponents. $(a \times 10^m)(b \times 10^n) = ab \times 10^{m+n}$
- **Division**: Divide the coefficients, subtract the exponents. $\frac{a \times 10^m}{b \times 10^n} = \frac{a}{b} \times 10^{m-n}$
- **Addition/Subtraction**: Convert to the same power of 10 first, then add/subtract coefficients.

---

### 3.35 Finance Formulas

#### 3.35.1 Simple Interest

$$A = P(1 + rt)$$

where:
- $A$ = total amount after time $t$
- $P$ = principal (initial amount)
- $r$ = annual interest rate (as a decimal)
- $t$ = time in years

**Simple interest** is calculated only on the original principal, not on accumulated interest.

#### 3.35.2 Compound Interest

$$A = P\left(1 + \frac{r}{n}\right)^{nt}$$

where:
- $A$ = total amount after time $t$
- $P$ = principal
- $r$ = annual interest rate (as a decimal)
- $n$ = number of times interest is compounded per year
- $t$ = time in years

**Special case — annually compounded**: $A = P(1 + r)^t$

**Key insight**: Compound interest grows faster than simple interest because interest earns interest. The more frequently interest is compounded (larger $n$), the greater the final amount.

#### 3.35.3 Commission and Total Cost Models

**Commission**: A percentage of sales, calculated as $\text{Commission} = \text{Rate} \times \text{Sales}$.

**Total Cost Models**: $\text{Total Cost} = \text{Fixed Cost} + (\text{Variable Cost per Unit} \times \text{Number of Units})$

This is essentially a linear function where the fixed cost is the y-intercept and the variable cost per unit is the slope.

---

### 3.36 Temperature Conversion

**Fahrenheit to Celsius**: $C = \frac{5}{9}(F - 32)$

**Celsius to Fahrenheit**: $F = \frac{9}{5}C + 32$

**Approximation**: $F \approx 2C + 32$ (double Celsius and add 32)

This approximation is reasonably accurate near typical room temperatures but becomes less precise at extreme temperatures. For example:
- At $C = 20$: Exact $F = 68$, Approx $F = 72$ (off by 4 degrees)
- At $C = 0$: Exact $F = 32$, Approx $F = 32$ (exact)
- At $C = 37$ (body temperature): Exact $F = 98.6$, Approx $F = 106$ (off by 7.4 degrees)

---

## Part V: Connecting the Concepts — A Unified Perspective

### 3.37 The Big Picture: Linear vs. Quadratic

| Property | Linear Function | Quadratic Function |
|---|---|---|
| **Form** | $y = mx + b$ | $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ |
| **Graph** | Straight line | Parabola |
| **Rate of change** | Constant ($m$) | Changes at every point |
| **Number of roots** | 1 (or 0 for horizontal, or infinite for $y=0$) | 0, 1, or 2 |
| **Symmetry** | None (unless constant) | Symmetric about axis $x = h$ |
| **Extremes** | None (unbounded) | One maximum or one minimum (vertex) |
| **Domain** | $(-\infty, \infty)$ | $(-\infty, \infty)$ |
| **Range** | $(-\infty, \infty)$ (non-horizontal) | $[k, \infty)$ or $(-\infty, k]$ |

### 3.38 When to Use Each Tool

- **Use linear functions** when the problem involves a constant rate of change, a steady increase or decrease, or a straight-line relationship.
- **Use quadratic functions** when the problem involves area, projectile motion, optimization (finding maximum or minimum), or any situation where the rate of change itself is changing.
- **Use systems of equations** when two conditions must be satisfied simultaneously, such as finding the intersection of two lines or the break-even point between two pricing models.
- **Use factoring** when you need to find roots quickly, simplify rational expressions, or solve quadratic equations without the quadratic formula.
- **Use the quadratic formula** when factoring is difficult or impossible (irrational roots), or when you need exact answers.

### 3.39 Common Errors to Avoid

1. **Sign errors in point-slope form**: Remember it's $y - y_1 = m(x - x_1)$, with minus signs. A point $(-3, 5)$ gives $y - 5 = m(x + 3)$.

2. **Confusing "no solution" with "infinite solutions"**: No solution = parallel lines (contradiction). Infinite solutions = same line (identity).

3. **Forgetting that $a \neq 0$ in a quadratic**: If $a = 0$, the function is linear, not quadratic.

4. **Averaging speeds for average speed**: Average speed is total distance divided by total time, NOT the arithmetic mean of the speeds.

5. **Adding successive percent changes**: A 10% increase followed by a 10% decrease does NOT return to the original value.

6. **Forgetting to check for GCF first** when factoring: Always factor out the greatest common factor before applying any other factoring method.

7. **Confusing $-b/(2a)$ with $b/(2a)$**: The x-coordinate of the vertex is $-\frac{b}{2a}$, with the negative sign. This is one of the most frequently misremembered formulas in algebra.

8. **Incorrectly interpreting the discriminant**: Remember that $b^2 - 4ac = 0$ means ONE real root (a double root), not "no real roots."

9. **Dividing by zero when finding slope**: If two points have the same x-coordinate, the slope is undefined (vertical line). You cannot divide by zero.

10. **Assuming all quadratics can be factored over integers**: Many quadratics have irrational roots and cannot be factored using integers. The quadratic formula always works.

---

### 3.40 Algebraic Reasoning and the "As a Function Of" Framework

Throughout all of algebra, the ability to express one quantity in terms of another is fundamental. When a problem says "express $A$ as a function of $r$," it means:

- $r$ is the independent variable (input, $x$)
- $A$ is the dependent variable (output, $y$)
- The answer should be in the form $A(r) = \text{expression involving } r$

This framework appears in geometry (area as a function of side length), physics (position as a function of time), economics (cost as a function of units produced), and countless other contexts. The key skill is identifying which quantity depends on which, and then using the relevant formula to express that dependency.

---

### 3.41 The Interplay of Algebra and Geometry

One of the most beautiful aspects of the mathematics covered in this chapter is the deep connection between algebraic equations and geometric graphs:

- Every linear equation $y = mx + b$ corresponds to a unique line in the plane, and every line in the plane (except vertical lines) corresponds to a unique linear equation.
- Every quadratic equation $y = ax^2 + bx + c$ corresponds to a unique parabola, and every parabola (opening up or down) corresponds to a unique quadratic equation.
- The **solutions** to a system of equations correspond to the **intersection points** of the graphs.
- The **roots** of a quadratic equation correspond to the **x-intercepts** of the parabola.
- The **vertex** of the parabola corresponds to the **maximum or minimum value** of the quadratic function.

This algebraic-geometric duality means that you can often solve a problem either algebraically (by manipulating equations) or geometrically (by analyzing graphs), and both approaches should give you the same answer. When stuck on an algebraic problem, try sketching a graph. When stuck on a geometric problem, try writing the relevant equations.

---

*This chapter has provided a comprehensive, rigorous treatment of linear functions, quadratic functions, systems of equations, and the algebraic reasoning skills that connect them. Mastery of these topics — including the ability to move fluently between different forms, interpret results in context, and avoid common errors — is essential for success in all subsequent mathematics and for performance on any standardized assessment that includes algebraic content.*

---


# Chapter 4: Geometry and Trigonometry — Theorems, Formulas, and Spatial Reasoning

---

## 4.1 The Foundations of Geometric Reasoning

Geometry, derived from the Greek *geo* (earth) and *metron* (measurement), is the branch of mathematics concerned with the properties, measurement, and relationships of points, lines, angles, surfaces, and solids. Before diving into formulas and theorems, it is essential to understand the **primitive (undefined) terms** upon which the entire logical structure of geometry is built:

- **Point**: A location in space with no dimension (no length, width, or thickness). Represented by a dot and labeled with a capital letter (e.g., point $A$).
- **Line**: A collection of points extending infinitely in two opposite directions. Has length but no width. A line can be named by two points on it (e.g., line $AB$ or $\overleftrightarrow{AB}$).
- **Plane**: A flat, two-dimensional surface that extends infinitely in all directions. Has length and width but no thickness. Named by three non-collinear points or a single capital letter (e.g., Plane $P$).

From these undefined terms, we derive **defined terms**: segment, ray, angle, circle, polygon, and so forth. The entire edifice of Euclidean geometry rests on a small set of **postulates** (assumptions accepted without proof) and **theorems** (statements proven from postulates and previously proven theorems).

### The Logical Structure: Axioms, Postulates, and Theorems

Geometry is not merely a collection of facts — it is a **deductive system**. Every theorem is derived logically from a small set of initial assumptions:

1. **Undefined terms** (point, line, plane) serve as the primitive building blocks.
2. **Postulates** (axioms) are the initial assumptions about these undefined terms that are accepted without proof. Euclid's five postulates are the most famous example.
3. **Definitions** create new terms from undefined terms and previously defined terms.
4. **Theorems** are statements that are logically proved using postulates, definitions, and previously proven theorems.
5. **Corollaries** are theorems that follow directly from a previously proven theorem with minimal additional proof.
6. **Lemmas** (auxiliary theorems) are smaller results proved in service of a larger theorem's proof.

This hierarchical structure means that every geometric fact can, in principle, be traced back to the postulates. Understanding this structure is essential for geometric reasoning: when you are stuck on a problem, ask yourself, "What postulates and theorems have I already established, and what remains to be shown?"

---

## 4.2 Lines and Angles

### 4.2.1 Types of Lines

| Type | Description |
|---|---|
| **Line** | Extends infinitely in both directions. |
| **Line Segment** | A portion of a line with two endpoints. |
| **Ray** | A portion of a line with one endpoint, extending infinitely in one direction. |
| **Parallel Lines** | Two lines in the same plane that never intersect. Denoted $\ell \parallel m$. |
| **Perpendicular Lines** | Two lines that intersect at a $90^\circ$ angle. Denoted $\ell \perp m$. |
| **Skew Lines** | Lines that are not in the same plane and do not intersect (3D geometry). |

### 4.2.2 Angle Classification

An **angle** is formed by two rays sharing a common endpoint (the **vertex**). Angles are measured in **degrees** ($^\circ$).

| Angle Type | Measure |
|---|---|
| **Acute** | $0^\circ < \theta < 90^\circ$ |
| **Right** | $\theta = 90^\circ$ |
| **Obtuse** | $90^\circ < \theta < 180^\circ$ |
| **Straight** | $\theta = 180^\circ$ |
| **Reflex** | $180^\circ < \theta < 360^\circ$ |
| **Full Rotation** | $\theta = 360^\circ$ |

### 4.2.3 Angle Relationships

When two lines intersect or when a transversal cuts through parallel lines, specific angle relationships emerge:

- **Complementary Angles**: Two angles whose measures sum to $90^\circ$. Each angle is the *complement* of the other.
- **Supplementary Angles**: Two angles whose measures sum to $180^\circ$. Each angle is the *supplement* of the other.
- **Adjacent Angles**: Two angles that share a common vertex and a common side but do not overlap.
- **Linear Pair**: Two adjacent angles whose non-common sides form a straight line. A linear pair is always supplementary (sums to $180^\circ$).
- **Vertical (Opposite) Angles**: Angles opposite each other when two lines intersect. **Vertical angles are always congruent** (equal in measure).

### 4.2.4 Angles Formed by a Transversal

When a **transversal** (a line crossing two or more lines) intersects two parallel lines, eight angles are formed with the following relationships:

- **Corresponding Angles**: Angles in matching positions at each intersection. **Corresponding angles are congruent** when lines are parallel.
- **Alternate Interior Angles**: Angles on opposite sides of the transversal and inside the two lines. **Alternate interior angles are congruent** when lines are parallel.
- **Alternate Exterior Angles**: Angles on opposite sides of the transversal and outside the two lines. **Alternate exterior angles are congruent** when lines are parallel.
- **Same-Side (Consecutive) Interior Angles**: Angles on the same side of the transversal and inside the two lines. **Same-side interior angles are supplementary** (sum to $180^\circ$) when lines are parallel.

> **Critical Reasoning Note**: The converses of these statements are also true. If corresponding angles are congruent, then the lines are parallel. This is the foundation for proving lines parallel in coordinate and Euclidean geometry.

### 4.2.5 Advanced Angle Relationships: The Angle Addition Postulate

The **Angle Addition Postulate** states that if point $B$ lies in the interior of $\angle AOC$, then:

$$m\angle AOB + m\angle BOC = m\angle AOC$$

This deceptively simple postulate is the foundation for solving many angle problems involving adjacent angles sharing a common vertex and side.

---

## 4.3 Triangles: The Fundamental Polygon

Triangles are the simplest and most important polygons in geometry. Every polygon can be decomposed into triangles, making triangle properties the gateway to understanding all polygonal geometry.

### 4.3.1 Classification of Triangles

**By Sides:**

| Type | Description |
|---|---|
| **Scalene** | No congruent sides; no congruent angles. |
| **Isosceles** | At least two congruent sides; two congruent base angles. |
| **Equilateral** | All three sides congruent; all three angles $= 60^\circ$. |

**By Angles:**

| Type | Description |
|---|---|
| **Acute** | All three angles are acute ($< 90^\circ$). |
| **Right** | One angle $= 90^\circ$; the other two are complementary. |
| **Obtuse** | One angle is obtuse ($> 90^\circ$). |

### 4.3.2 Solved Example: Triangle Angle Ratios

**Problem**: The angles of a triangle are in the ratio 4:5:6. Find the measure of each angle.

**Solution**: Let the angles be $4x$, $5x$, and $6x$. Since the sum of angles in a triangle is $180^\circ$:

$$4x + 5x + 6x = 180$$
$$15x = 180$$
$$x = 12$$

The angles are $48^\circ$, $60^\circ$, and $72^\circ$ respectively.

---

## 4.4 Area and Perimeter

### 4.4.1 Perimeter

The **perimeter** of a polygon is the sum of the lengths of its sides.

| Shape | Perimeter Formula |
|---|---|
| **Square** | $P = 4s$ |
| **Rectangle** | $P = 2(\ell + w)$ |
| **Triangle** | $P = a + b + c$ |
| **Parallelogram** | $P = 2(a + b)$ |
| **Regular Polygon** | $P = n \cdot s$ ($n$ = number of sides, $s$ = side length) |

### 4.4.2 Area Formulas

| Shape | Area Formula | Variables |
|---|---|---|
| **Triangle** | $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$ | $b$ = base, $h$ = height (altitude) |
| **Triangle (sine formula)** | $A = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin(C)$ | $a, b$ = two sides, $C$ = included angle |
| **Triangle (Heron's Formula)** | $A = \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$ | $s = \frac{a+b+c}{2}$ (semiperimeter) |
| **Equilateral Triangle** | $A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4}$ | $s$ = side length |
| **Square** | $A = s^2$ | $s$ = side length |
| **Rectangle** | $A = \ell w$ | $\ell$ = length, $w$ = width |
| **Parallelogram** | $A = bh$ | $b$ = base, $h$ = height |
| **Trapezoid** | $A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$ | $b_1, b_2$ = bases, $h$ = height |
| **Rhombus** | $A = \frac{1}{2}d_1d_2$ | $d_1, d_2$ = diagonals |
| **Regular Polygon** | $A = \frac{1}{2}ap$ | $a$ = apothem, $p$ = perimeter |
| **Circle** | $A = \pi r^2$ | $r$ = radius |
| **Sector of a Circle** | $A = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi r^2$ | $\theta$ = central angle in degrees |

> **Key Insight**: The height (altitude) of a shape is always measured **perpendicular** to the base. In a triangle, the height may fall outside the triangle (for obtuse triangles). In a parallelogram, the height is not the length of the slanted side.

---

## 4.5 Solved Examples: Geometry in Action

### Example 1: Triangle with Altitude outside the Triangle

**Problem**: In $\triangle ABC$, $\angle B$ is obtuse. The altitude from $A$ to side $BC$ (extended) has length 6. If $BC = 8$ and $BD = 5$ (where $D$ is the foot of the altitude on the extension of $BC$), find the area of $\triangle ABC$.

**Solution**:

The area formula for a triangle is $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$, where the height must be perpendicular to the base.
Let the base be $BC = 8$ and the height (altitude from $A$ to line $BC$) be $h = 6$.

$$A = \frac{1}{2} \times 8 \times 6 = 24 \text{ square units}$$

Note: The fact that the altitude falls outside the obtuse triangle does not change the area calculation. The altitude of 6 is simply the perpendicular distance from $A$ to line $BC$, regardless of where the foot $D$ falls relative to segment $BC$.

---

### Example 2: Area of an Equilateral Triangle

**Problem**: An equilateral triangle has a side length of 8 cm. Find its area.

**Solution**:

Using the equilateral triangle area formula:

$$A = \frac{s^2\sqrt{3}}{4} = \frac{8^2\sqrt{3}}{4} = \frac{64\sqrt{3}}{4} = 16\sqrt{3} \approx 27.71 \text{ cm}^2$$

Alternatively, we can derive this by finding the height first. Drawing an altitude from one vertex to the opposite side splits the equilateral triangle into two $30^\circ\text{-}60^\circ\text{-}90^\circ$ right triangles. The altitude is the long leg:

$$h = \frac{s\sqrt{3}}{2} = \frac{8\sqrt{3}}{2} = 4\sqrt{3}$$
$$A = \frac{1}{2} \times 8 \times 4\sqrt{3} = 16\sqrt{3}$$

Both methods confirm $A = 16\sqrt{3} \approx 27.71 \text{ cm}^2$.

---

### Example 3: Finding the Height of a Parallelogram

**Problem**: A parallelogram has an area of $72 \text{ cm}^2$ and a base of $9 \text{ cm}$. Find the height corresponding to this base.

**Solution**:

$$A = bh$$
$$72 = 9h$$
$$h = 8 \text{ cm}$$

The height is $8 \text{ cm}$. Note: the height of $8 \text{ cm}$ is NOT necessarily the length of the adjacent side of the parallelogram — it is the perpendicular distance between bases.

---

### Example 4: Missing Base of a Trapezoid

**Problem**: A trapezoid has an area of $96 \text{ cm}^2$, a height of $8 \text{ cm}$, and one base measuring $10 \text{ cm}$ ...

**Solution**:

$$A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h$$
$$96 = \frac{1}{2}(10 + b_2) \times 8$$
$$96 = 4(10 + b_2)$$
$$24 = 10 + b_2$$
$$b_2 = 14 \text{ cm}$$

The second base measures $14 \text{ cm}$.

---

### Example 5: Sector-shaped Garden

**Problem**: A garden is shaped like a sector of a circle with radius $r = 15$ meters and a central angle of $72^\circ$. Find the area of the garden and its arc length.

**Solution**:

**Sector area**:

$$A = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi r^2 = \frac{72}{360} \cdot \pi \cdot 15^2 = \frac{1}{5} \cdot 225\pi = 45\pi \approx 141.37 \text{ m}^2$$

**Arc length**:

$$s = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot 2\pi r = \frac{72}{360} \cdot 30\pi = \frac{1}{5} \cdot 30\pi = 6\pi \approx 18.85 \text{ m}$$

---

### Example 6: Change in Area of a Circle

**Problem**: A circle initially has radius $r$. After the radius is doubled, by what percentage does the area increase?

**Solution**:

Original area: $A_1 = \pi r^2$
New radius: $2r$
New area: $A_2 = \pi(2r)^2 = 4\pi r^2$

$$\text{Factor of change} = \frac{A_2}{A_1} = \frac{4\pi r^2}{\pi r^2} = 4$$

The area increases by a factor of 4, which is a **300% increase** (the new area is 400% of the original).

---

## 4.5 Circles

### 4.5.1 Fundamental Definitions

- **Circle**: The set of all points in a plane equidistant from a given point (the **center**).
- **Radius** ($r$): A segment from the center to any point on the circle.
- **Diameter** ($d$): A segment passing through the center with endpoints on the circle. $d = 2r$.
- **Chord**: A segment whose endpoints lie on the circle.
- **Tangent**: A line that intersects the circle at exactly one point (the **point of tangency**). The radius to the point of tangency is perpendicular to the tangent line.
- **Secant**: A line that intersects the circle at two points.
- **Arc**: A continuous part of the circle. A **semicircle** measures $180^\circ$. A **minor arc** measures less than $180^\circ$. A **major arc** measures more than $180^\circ$.
- **Central Angle**: An angle whose vertex is at the center of the circle. The measure of a central angle equals the measure of its intercepted arc.
- **Inscribed Angle**: An angle whose vertex is on the circle. The measure of an inscribed angle equals **half** the measure of its intercepted arc.

### 4.5.2 Circle Theorems

**Inscribed Angle Theorem**: The measure of an inscribed angle is half the measure of its intercepted arc.

$$m\angle = \frac{1}{2} \cdot m(\text{intercepted arc})$$

**Corollary**: Inscribed angles that intercept the same arc are congruent.

**Corollary**: An angle inscribed in a semicircle is a right angle ($90^\circ$). This means any triangle inscribed in a circle where one side is the diameter is a right triangle.

**Tangent-Chord Theorem**: The angle formed by a tangent and a chord equals half the measure of the intercepted arc.

**Chord-Chord Angle**: When two chords intersect inside a circle, the measure of the angle formed equals half the sum of the measures of the intercepted arcs.

$$m\angle = \frac{1}{2}(m\text{arc}_1 + m\text{arc}_2)$$

**Secant-Secant Angle (outside the circle)**: When two secants intersect outside a circle, the measure of the angle formed equals half the **difference** of the measures of the intercepted arcs.

$$m\angle = \frac{1}{2}(m\text{major arc} - m\text{minor arc})$$

### 4.5.3 Circle Measurements

**Circumference**: $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$

**Area**: $A = \pi r^2$

**Area of a Sector**: $A = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi r^2$ (where $\theta$ is in degrees)

**Arc Length**: $s = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot 2\pi r = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi d$

### 4.5.4 Equation of a Circle (Coordinate Geometry)

The standard form equation of a circle with center $(h, k)$ and radius $r$:

$$(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$$

The center is at $(h, k)$ and the radius is $r$.

> **Example**: $(x - 3)^2 + (y + 2)^2 = 25$ represents a circle with center $(3, -2)$ and radius $5$.

### 4.5.5 Solved Example: Circle Sector and Arc Length

**Problem**: A circle of radius $12$ cm contains a sector with central angle $80^\circ$. Find the area of the sector and the arc length.

**Solution**:

**Sector area**:

$$A = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi r^2 = \frac{80}{360} \cdot \pi \cdot 12^2 = \frac{2}{9} \cdot 144\pi = 32\pi \approx 100.53 \text{ cm}^2$$

**Arc length**:

$$s = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot 2\pi r = \frac{80}{360} \cdot 2 \cdot 12\pi = \frac{2}{9} \cdot 24\pi = \frac{16\pi}{3} \approx 16.76 \text{ cm}$$

---

## 4.6 Polygons

### 4.6.1 Angle Sums

**Sum of Interior Angles of a Polygon**: For any polygon with $n$ sides:

$$\text{Sum of interior angles} = (n - 2) \times 180^\circ$$

| Polygon | Sides ($n$) | Sum of Interior Angles |
|---|---|---|
| Triangle | 3 | $180^\circ$ |
| Quadrilateral | 4 | $360^\circ$ |
| Pentagon | 5 | $540^\circ$ |
| Hexagon | 6 | $720^\circ$ |
| Heptagon | 7 | $900^\circ$ |
| Octagon | 8 | $1080^\circ$ |
| Decagon | 10 | $1440^\circ$ |

**Sum of Exterior Angles of Any Polygon**: The sum of the exterior angles of any convex polygon, one at each vertex, is always $360^\circ$.

**Each Exterior Angle of a Regular Polygon**: $\frac{360^\circ}{n}$

**Each Interior Angle of a Regular Polygon**: $\frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$ or equivalently $180^\circ - \frac{360^\circ}{n}$

### 4.6.2 Solved Example: Regular Polygon Interior Angle

**Problem**: Find the measure of each interior angle of a regular dodecagon ($12$ sides).

**Solution**:

$$\text{Interior angle} = \frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n} = \frac{(12-2) \times 180^\circ}{12} = \frac{10 \times 180^\circ}{12} = \frac{1800^\circ}{12} = 150^\circ$$

Each interior angle of a regular dodecagon measures $150^\circ$.

---

### 4.6.3 Solving Polygon Side Count from Interior Angles

**Problem**: Each interior angle of a regular convex polygon measures $140^\circ$. How many sides does the polygon have?

**Solution**:

$$\frac{(n-2) \times 180}{n} = 140$$
$$(n-2) \times 180 = 140n$$
$$180n - 360 = 140n$$
$$40n = 360$$
$$n = 9$$

The polygon is a **nonagon** (9-sided polygon).

---

## 4.7 Quadrilaterals

A **quadrilateral** is a four-sided polygon. The family of quadrilaterals has a hierarchical structure based on their properties and the relationships between their sides and angles.

### 4.7.1 The Quadrilateral Hierarchy

At the top of the hierarchy is the **quadrilateral** — any four-sided polygon. Below it, increasingly specific families are defined by additional properties:

1. **Trapezoid**: A quadrilateral with at least one pair of parallel sides. The parallel sides are called **bases**, the non-parallel sides are called **legs**, and the distance between the bases is called the **height**.
2. **Parallelogram**: A quadrilateral with both pairs of opposite sides parallel. It's a special case of a trapezoid where there are *two* pairs of parallel sides.
3. **Rectangle**: A parallelogram with four right angles. This additional condition forces the diagonals to be congruent.
4. **Rhombus**: A parallelogram with four congruent sides. This additional condition forces the diagonals to be perpendicular and to bisect the interior angles.
5. **Square**: A quadrilateral that meets all the conditions of both a rectangle and a rhombus — it has four right angles and four congruent sides. This is the most specific quadrilateral in the hierarchy.

```
Quadrilateral
└── Trapezoid (≥1 pair of parallel sides)
    └── Parallelogram (2 pairs of parallel sides)
        ├── Rectangle (+ four right angles)
        │   └── Square (+ four congruent sides)
        └── Rhombus (+ four congruent sides)
            └── Square (+ four right angles)
```

### 4.7.2 Properties of a Parallelogram

A parallelogram is defined as a quadrilateral with both pairs of opposite sides parallel. From this definition, several important properties follow:

| Property | Description |
|---|---|
| Opposite sides | Parallel and congruent |
| Opposite angles | Congruent |
| Consecutive angles | Supplementary (sum to $180^\circ$) |
| Diagonals | Bisect each other |

Each property provides essential tools for solving geometry problems involving parallelograms. For example, knowing that consecutive angles are supplementary allows you to find all angles when only one is given.

### 4.7.3 Ways to Prove a Quadrilateral is a Parallelogram

To prove a quadrilateral is a parallelogram, you only need to show that **any one** of the following conditions is met:

- Both pairs of opposite sides are parallel (the definition).
- Both pairs of opposite sides are congruent.
- Both pairs of opposite angles are congruent.
- Diagonals bisect each other.
- One pair of opposite sides is both parallel and congruent.

Each condition provides a different pathway to the same conclusion, and choosing the most efficient one depends on the given information in the problem.

---

### 4.7.4 Properties of Special Parallelograms

**Rectangle Properties** (all parallelogram properties PLUS):

| Property | Description |
|---|---|
| All angles | $90^\circ$ |
| Diagonals | Congruent |

**Rhombus Properties** (all parallelogram properties PLUS):

| Property | Description |
|---|---|
| All sides | Congruent |
| Diagonals | Perpendicular |
| Diagonals | Bisect interior angles |

**Square Properties** (all properties of BOTH rectangle and rhombus):

| Property | Description |
|---|---|
| All sides | Congruent |
| All angles | $90^\circ$ |
| Diagonals | Congruent, perpendicular, bisect angles |

### 4.7.5 Solved Example: Isosceles Trapezoid

**Problem**: An isosceles trapezoid has bases of $10\text{ m}$ and $18\text{ m}$. If each leg measures $5\text{ m}$, find the height and area.

**Solution**: In an isosceles trapezoid, the legs are congruent, and the base angles are congruent. To find the height, we can drop perpendiculars from the shorter base to the longer base, creating two right triangles on the sides.

The difference in base lengths is $18 - 10 = 8\text{ m}$, which is split equally on both sides: $4\text{ m}$ each.

Each right triangle has:
- Base: $4\text{ m}$
- Hypotenuse: $5\text{ m}$
- Height: $h$

By the Pythagorean theorem:

$$h = \sqrt{5^2 - 4^2} = \sqrt{25 - 16} = \sqrt{9} = 3\text{ m}$$

Now, the area:

$$A = \frac{1}{2}(b_1 + b_2)h = \frac{1}{2}(10 + 18)(3) = \frac{1}{2}(28)(3) = 42\text{ m}^2$$

**Answer**: The height is $3\text{ m}$ and the area is $42\text{ m}^2$.

---

### 4.7.6 Trapezoid Median and Kite Properties

**Trapezoid Median (Midsegment)**: The segment connecting the midpoints of the legs is:

- Parallel to the bases
- Equal in length to the average of the bases:

$$m = \frac{b_1 + b_2}{2}$$

This property is frequently tested because it connects the midsegment to a simple average of the two bases.

**Kite Properties**:

| Property | Description |
|---|---|
| Sides | Two pairs of consecutive congruent sides |
| Diagonals | Perpendicular |
| One diagonal | Bisects the other |
| One pair of opposite angles | Congruent |

A kite is distinct from a parallelogram — rather than having opposite sides congruent, it has adjacent sides congruent. This creates perpendicular diagonals rather than diagonals that bisect each other.

### 4.7.7 Diagonals of Polygons

The number of diagonals in a polygon with $n$ sides:

$$\text{Number of diagonals} = \frac{n(n-3)}{2}$$

This formula comes from the fact that each of the $n$ vertices can connect to $n - 3$ other vertices (all except itself and its two adjacent vertices), and dividing by 2 accounts for counting each diagonal twice.

---

## 4.8 Solid Geometry (3D Figures)

### 4.8.1 Volume Formulas

| Shape | Volume Formula | Variables |
|---|---|---|
| **Rectangular Prism** | $V = \ell wh$ | $\ell$ = length, $w$ = width, $h$ = height |
| **Cube** | $V = s^3$ | $s$ = side length |
| **Cylinder (right circular)** | $V = \pi r^2 h$ | $r$ = radius, $h$ = height |
| **Cone** | $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$ | $r$ = radius, $h$ = height |
| **Sphere** | $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ | $r$ = radius |
| **Pyramid** | $V = \frac{1}{3}Bh$ | $B$ = area of base, $h$ = height |
| **Prism (general)** | $V = Bh$ | $B$ = area of base, $h$ = height |

### 4.8.2 Surface Area Formulas

| Shape | Surface Area Formula |
|---|---|
| **Rectangular Prism** | $SA = 2(\ell w + \ell h + wh)$ |
| **Cube** | $SA = 6s^2$ |
| **Cylinder (right circular)** | $SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh$ (two circular bases + lateral area) |
| **Cone** | $SA = \pi r^2 + \pi r\ell$ (base + lateral area, $\ell$ = slant height) |
| **Sphere** | $SA = 4\pi r^2$ |

### 4.8.3 The Factor of Change in 3D Figures

When dimensions of a solid figure change, the volume changes according to the **product** of the factors of change for each dimension. This principle extends the factor-of-change concept from 2D to 3D:

- **Cone**: $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$. If radius decreases by $20\%$ (factor $= 0.8$) and height increases by $10\%$ (factor $= 1.1$):

$$V_{\text{new}} = (0.8)^2(1.1)V = 0.64 \times 1.1 \times V = 0.704V$$

This represents a $29.6\%$ decrease in volume.

- **Sphere**: $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$. If the radius doubles, the volume increases by a factor of $2^3 = 8$.

> **General Principle**: For any 3D figure, if all linear dimensions are scaled by a factor $k$, the volume scales by $k^3$ and the surface area scales by $k^2$.

### 4.8.4 Solved Example: Rate of Water Level Rise

**Problem**: Water is poured into an empty inverted right circular cone at a constant rate of $15\text{ cm}^3/\text{s}$. The cone has a height of $40\text{ cm}$ and a base radius of $20\text{ cm}$. At what rate is the water level rising when the water depth is $10\text{ cm}$?

**Solution**:

This is a **related rates** problem that combines geometry with calculus. Let's find the relationship between the volume of water and the water depth.

Because the cone is inverted (vertex at the bottom), the water forms a smaller cone similar to the original cone. The ratio of radius to height of the original cone is:

$$\frac{r}{h} = \frac{20}{40} = \frac{1}{2}$$

So at any water depth $h$, the water surface radius is $r = \frac{h}{2}$.

The volume of water at depth $h$:

$$V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h = \frac{1}{3}\pi \left(\frac{h}{2}\right)^2 h = \frac{1}{3}\pi \cdot \frac{h^2}{4} \cdot h = \frac{\pi h^3}{12}$$

Differentiating both sides with respect to time $t$:

$$\frac{dV}{dt} = \frac{\pi}{12} \cdot 3h^2 \cdot \frac{dh}{dt} = \frac{\pi h^2}{4} \cdot \frac{dh}{dt}$$

At the moment when $h = 10$ cm and $\frac{dV}{dt} = 15\text{ cm}^3/\text{s}$:

$$15 = \frac{\pi (10)^2}{4} \cdot \frac{dh}{dt} = \frac{100\pi}{4} \cdot \frac{dh}{dt} = 25\pi \cdot \frac{dh}{dt}$$

$$\frac{dh}{dt} = \frac{15}{25\pi} = \frac{3}{5\pi} \approx 0.191\text{ cm/s}$$

The water level is rising at a rate of $\frac{3}{5\pi} \approx 0.191\text{ cm/s}$ when the depth is $10$ cm.

---

### 4.8.5 Solved Example: Surface Area of a Cylinder

**Problem**: A cylinder has a radius of $6\text{ cm}$ and a height of $15\text{ cm}$. Find its total surface area.

**Solution**:

$$SA = 2\pi r^2 + 2\pi rh = 2\pi(6)^2 + 2\pi(6)(15)$$
$$= 2\pi(36) + 180\pi = 72\pi + 180\pi = 252\pi \approx 791.68\text{ cm}^2$$

---

### 4.8.6 Solved Example: Volume of a Sphere

**Problem**: A sphere has a diameter of $18\text{ cm}$. Find its volume and surface area.

**Solution**: The radius is $r = 9$ cm.

**Volume**:

$$V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3 = \frac{4}{3}\pi(9)^3 = \frac{4}{3}\pi \cdot 729 = 972\pi \approx 3053.63\text{ cm}^3$$

**Surface Area**:

$$SA = 4\pi r^2 = 4\pi(9)^2 = 324\pi \approx 1017.88\text{ cm}^2$$

---

## 4.9 Coordinate Geometry

### 4.9.1 The Coordinate Plane

The Cartesian coordinate plane is divided into four **quadrants** by the $x$-axis and $y$-axis:

| Quadrant | $x$-coordinate | $y$-coordinate |
|---|---|---|
| **I** | Positive ($+$) | Positive ($+$) |
| **II** | Negative ($-$) | Positive ($+$) |
| **III** | Negative ($-$) | Negative ($-$) |
| **IV** | Positive ($+$) | Negative ($-$) |

### 4.9.2 Distance Formula

The distance between two points $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$:

$$d = \sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}$$

This is derived directly from the Pythagorean Theorem, where $(x_2 - x_1)$ and $(y_2 - y_1)$ represent the horizontal and vertical legs of a right triangle, and the distance is the hypotenuse.

### 4.9.3 Midpoint Formula

The midpoint of the segment connecting $(x_1, y_1)$ and $(x_2, y_2)$:

$$M = \left(\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1 + y_2}{2}\right)$$

The midpoint is the **average** of the $x$-coordinates and the **average** of the $y$-coordinates. This follows from the fact that the midpoint divides the segment into two equal parts.

### 4.9.4 Solved Example: Distance and Midpoint

**Problem**: Two points $A$ and $B$ have coordinates $(1, 4)$ and $(7, 10)$ respectively. Find the distance $AB$ and the midpoint of segment $AB$.

**Solution**:

**Distance**:

$$d = \sqrt{(7-1)^2 + (10-4)^2} = \sqrt{6^2 + 6^2} = \sqrt{36 + 36} = \sqrt{72} = 6\sqrt{2}$$

**Midpoint**:

$$M = \left(\frac{1+7}{2}, \frac{4+10}{2}\right) = (4, 7)$$

---

## 4.10 Strategic Notes for Geometric Problem-Solving

1. **Draw a diagram.** If one is not provided, sketch one immediately. Label all known information. Visual representation transforms abstract statements into concrete spatial relationships.

2. **Mark givens on the diagram.** Transfer every piece of given information — side lengths, angle measures, parallel lines — onto your visual representation. This prevents you from losing track of information.

3. **Look for hidden relationships.** Inscribed angles, vertical angles, alternate interior angles, and similar triangles are often embedded in complex figures. Recognizing these relationships is the key to unlocking difficult problems.

4. **Add auxiliary lines.** Sometimes drawing an altitude, a diagonal, or an extension of a side reveals a solvable right triangle or a pair of similar triangles. This is one of the most powerful techniques in Euclidean geometry.

5. **Exploit symmetry.** Many geometric problems have symmetric properties — equal sides, equal angles, reflective or rotational symmetry — that simplify calculations dramatically. Identifying symmetry often reduces a complex problem to a simpler one.

6. **Work backwards.** In proof-based problems, start from what you need to prove and identify what would be sufficient to establish it. This reverse reasoning often reveals the path forward.

7. **Check units and scale.** Ensure all measurements are in the same units before computing. Be alert for scale factors in similar figures — a figure drawn at $1:2$ scale has dimensions that differ from the actual figure.

8. **Use the factor of change approach.** When a dimension changes by a percentage, convert to a multiplicative factor and apply it to the relevant formula. This avoids the common error of directly adding or subtracting percentages in area problems (a 10% increase in the side of a square does NOT produce a 10% increase in area — it produces a 21% increase).

9. **Memorize the special right triangles.** The 30-60-90 and 45-45-90 ratios appear in an enormous variety of problems. Instant recognition saves time and reduces errors.

10. **Verify triangle existence.** Before solving, confirm that given side lengths satisfy the Triangle Inequality Theorem. If they can't form a triangle at all, no solution exists.

---

## 4.11 Key Formulas Summary Sheet

### Triangles
- Sum of interior angles: $180^\circ$
- Pythagorean Theorem: $a^2 + b^2 = c^2$
- Area: $A = \frac{1}{2}bh$, $A = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin C$, Heron's formula
- Special right triangles: $s:s:s\sqrt{2}$ (45-45-90), $s:s\sqrt{3}:2s$ (30-60-90)

### Polygons
- Sum of interior angles: $(n-2) \times 180^\circ$
- Sum of exterior angles: $360^\circ$
- Each interior angle of regular $n$-gon: $\frac{(n-2) \times 180^\circ}{n}$

### Circles
- Circumference: $C = 2\pi r = \pi d$
- Area: $A = \pi r^2$
- Arc length: $s = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot 2\pi r$ (degrees) or $s = r\theta$ (radians)
- Sector area: $A = \frac{\theta}{360} \cdot \pi r^2$ (degrees) or $A = \frac{1}{2}r^2\theta$ (radians)
- Equation: $(x-h)^2 + (y-k)^2 = r^2$

### Coordinate Geometry
- Distance: $d = \sqrt{(x_2-x_1)^2 + (y_2-y_1)^2}$
- Midpoint: $M = \left(\frac{x_1+x_2}{2}, \frac{y_1+y_2}{2}\right)$
- Slope: $m = \frac{y_2-y_1}{x_2-x_1}$

### Solid Geometry
- Cylinder volume: $V = \pi r^2 h$
- Cone volume: $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$
- Sphere volume: $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$
- Sphere surface area: $SA = 4\pi r^2$

---

*This chapter has provided a comprehensive, rigorous treatment of Euclidean geometry, coordinate geometry, and solid geometry. Mastery of these concepts — their definitions, theorems, formulas, and interconnections — is essential for success in any standardized assessment and for the development of spatial reasoning skills that extend far beyond the classroom. The solved examples demonstrate how these principles are applied in practice, and the strategic notes provide a framework for approaching any geometry problem with confidence.*

---


# Chapter 5: Ratios, Percents, Rates, and Factor-of-Change Problem-Solving Tactics

---

## 5.1 — The Architecture of Ratios: From Intuition to Formalism

### 5.1.1 — What a Ratio Truly Represents

A **ratio** is a multiplicative comparison between two or more quantities. It tells us *how many times* one quantity contains another—it does **not** tell us the actual values of the quantities involved. This is the single most important conceptual distinction in all of ratio work.

> **Key Principle:** A ratio of $3 : 5$ between apples and oranges means that for every 3 apples there are 5 oranges. It does **not** mean there are exactly 3 apples and 5 oranges. There could be 30 apples and 50 oranges, or 3,000 apples and 5,000 oranges. The ratio only constrains the *relationship*, not the *absolute amounts*.

This means that every ratio can be scaled up or down by multiplying or dividing both terms by the same nonzero constant. The ratio $3 : 5$ is equivalent to $6 : 10$, $9 : 15$, $30 : 50$, and so on. All of these represent the same proportional relationship.

### 5.1.2 — Three Ways to Express a Ratio

A ratio comparing quantity $a$ to quantity $b$ can be written in three equivalent forms:

| Form | Notation | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Colon form | $a : b$ | $3 : 5$ |
| Fraction form | $\frac{a}{b}$ | $\frac{3}{5}$ |
| Word form | "$a$ to $b$" | "3 to 5" |

Each form is useful in different contexts. The colon form is most common in word problems. The fraction form is most useful when performing algebraic manipulations. The word form is most useful for conceptual understanding.

### 5.1.3 — Part-to-Part vs. Part-to-Whole Ratios

This distinction is critical and is the source of many errors.

- **Part-to-part ratio** compares one part of a whole to another part. Example: The ratio of apples to oranges is $3 : 5$.
- **Part-to-whole ratio** compares one part to the entire collection. Example: The ratio of apples to total fruit is $3 : (3 + 5) = 3 : 8$.

When a problem says "the ratio of boys to girls is $4 : 7$," and then asks "what fraction of the class is boys?" you must convert from part-to-part to part-to-whole. The boys represent 4 parts out of a total of $4 + 7 = 11$ parts, so the fraction is $\frac{4}{11}$.

> **Common Trap:** A problem states the ratio of men to women is $2 : 3$ and asks what percentage of the group are women. The answer is $\frac{3}{5} = 60\%$ because the total parts are $2 + 3 = 5$, and women represent 3 of those 5 parts. But if the problem had said "the ratio of men to TOTAL PEOPLE is $2 : 3$," then men represent $\frac{2}{3}$ of the group. Always identify whether you are dealing with part-to-part or part-to-whole.

### 5.1.4 — The Multiplier Method (The "Parts" Technique)

This is the single most powerful technique for solving ratio problems algebraically.

**Procedure:**
1. Let the ratio $a : b : c$ be represented as $ax : bx : cx$, where $x$ is the **multiplier** (also called the "scaling factor").
2. Use the information given in the problem to determine the value of $x$.
3. Compute each quantity by multiplying the ratio number by $x$.

**Example framework:** If the ratio of three angles in a triangle is $2 : 3 : 5$, represent them as $2x$, $3x$, and $5x$. Since the sum of angles in a triangle is $180^\circ$, we have:

$$2x + 3x + 5x = 180$$

$$10x = 180$$

$$x = 18$$

The angles are $36^\circ$, $54^\circ$, and $90^\circ$.

The multiplier method transforms a purely proportional problem into a simple linear equation. It works for any number of quantities in a ratio and is universally applicable.

### 5.1.5 — Ratios Must Be Simplified

A ratio should always be expressed in lowest terms, just like a fraction. The ratio $6 : 10$ should be simplified to $3 : 5$ by dividing both terms by their greatest common factor (GCF), which is 2.

However, there is an important exception: **if the ratio involves units**, you may need to keep the numbers in their original form or convert to common units first. The ratio of 3 feet to 2 yards requires converting yards to feet (2 yards = 6 feet), giving $3 : 6 = 1 : 2$.

### 5.1.6 — Ratios Involving More Than Two Quantities

When three or more quantities are in ratio (e.g., $a : b : c$), the same principles apply. Represent them as $ax$, $bx$, $cx$. The total number of parts is $a + b + c$. Each quantity's fraction of the whole is its ratio number divided by the total number of parts.

**Combining ratios:** If $a : b = 2 : 3$ and $b : c = 4 : 5$, to find $a : b : c$, we need to make the $b$-terms equal. The LCM of 3 and 4 is 12. Scale the first ratio by 4: $a : b = 8 : 12$. Scale the second ratio by 3: $b : c = 12 : 15$. Therefore $a : b : c = 8 : 12 : 15$.

### 5.1.7 — Ratios Do Not Give Exact Values

This cannot be emphasized enough. If a problem tells you the ratio of boys to girls in a classroom is $3 : 4$, you cannot determine the number of boys or girls without additional information. The class could have 7 students (3 boys, 4 girls), 14 students (6 boys, 8 girls), 21 students, 28 students, etc. The class size must be a multiple of 7.

This leads to an important constraint: **in problems involving whole objects (people, books, cars), the multiplier $x$ must be a positive integer.** This constraint can be used to eliminate impossible answer choices.

---

## 5.2 — Proportions: Setting Ratios Equal

### 5.2.1 — Definition and Structure

A **proportion** is an equation stating that two ratios are equal:

$$\frac{a}{b} = \frac{c}{d}$$

This is read "$a$ is to $b$ as $c$ is to $d$." The terms $a$ and $d$ are called the **extremes**, and $b$ and $c$ are called the **means**.

### 5.2.2 — Cross-Multiplication (The Fundamental Theorem of Proportions)

If $\frac{a}{b} = \frac{c}{d}$, then $ad = bc$. This is the primary tool for solving proportions. The product of the means equals the product of the extremes.

**Procedure for solving a proportion:**
1. Cross-multiply to obtain an equation without fractions.
2. Solve the resulting equation for the unknown.
3. Check that the solution does not make any denominator zero.

### 5.2.3 — Setting Up Proportions from Word Problems

The key to setting up proportions correctly is **unit alignment**. The units in the numerators must match, and the units in the denominators must match.

**Framework:** "If 5 widgets cost $12, how much do 8 widgets cost?"

Set up: $\frac{5 \text{ widgets}}{\$12} = \frac{8 \text{ widgets}}{\$x}$

Cross-multiply: $5x = 96$, so $x = 19.2$. The answer is $19.20.

Alternatively: $\frac{\$12}{5 \text{ widgets}} = \frac{\$x}{8 \text{ widgets}}$

This gives the same result. The critical point is that the same units occupy the same position (numerator or denominator) in both ratios.

### 5.2.4 — Direct Variation (Direct Proportionality)

Two quantities $y$ and $x$ are in **direct variation** (or direct proportion) if their ratio is constant:

$$\frac{y}{x} = k \quad \text{or equivalently} \quad y = kx$$

where $k$ is the **constant of proportionality**.

**Characteristics:**
- As $x$ increases, $y$ increases proportionally.
- The graph is a straight line through the origin.
- If $x$ doubles, $y$ doubles. If $x$ triples, $y$ triples.

**Identifying direct variation in problems:** Look for phrases like "varies directly as," "is directly proportional to," or situations where doubling one quantity causes the other to double.

### 5.2.5 — Inverse Variation (Inverse Proportionality)

Two quantities $y$ and $x$ are in **inverse variation** if their product is constant:

$$xy = k \quad \text{or equivalently} \quad y = \frac{k}{x}$$

**Characteristics:**
- As $x$ increases, $y$ decreases proportionally.
- The graph is a hyperbola.
- If $x$ doubles, $y$ is halved. If $x$ triples, $y$ becomes one-third.

**Identifying inverse variation:** Look for phrases like "varies inversely as," "is inversely proportional to," or situations where increasing one quantity causes the other to decrease in a reciprocal manner. Classic examples include speed and time for a fixed distance (double the speed, halve the time), and number of workers and time to complete a fixed job.

### 5.2.6 — Joint and Combined Variation

More complex relationships involve multiple variables:

- **Joint variation:** $y$ varies jointly as $x$ and $z$ means $y = kxz$.
- **Combined variation:** $y$ varies directly as $x$ and inversely as $z$ means $y = \frac{kx}{z}$.

These are natural extensions of direct and inverse variation and are solved using the same constant-of-proportionality approach.

---

## 5.3 — Percents: The Language of Proportions

### 5.3.1 — The Meaning of Percent

The word **percent** comes from the Latin *per centum*, meaning "per hundred." A percent is a ratio with a denominator of 100. The symbol % means "/100" or "$\times \frac{1}{100}$."

- $25\% = \frac{25}{100} = 0.25 = \frac{1}{4}$
- $50\% = \frac{50}{100} = 0.50 = \frac{1}{2}$
- $33.33\% \approx \frac{1}{3}$
- $66.67\% \approx \frac{2}{3}$

### 5.3.2 — The Three Conversion Operations

Mastering percent problems requires fluency in three conversions:

| Conversion | Operation | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Percent → Decimal | Divide by 100 (move decimal 2 places left) | $45\% \to 0.45$ |
| Decimal → Percent | Multiply by 100 (move decimal 2 places right) | $0.075 \to 7.5\%$ |
| Percent → Fraction | Write over 100, then simplify | $60\% = \frac{60}{100} = \frac{3}{5}$ |
| Fraction → Percent | Divide, then multiply by 100 | $\frac{3}{8} = 0.375 = 37.5\%$ |

### 5.3.3 — The Three Percent Problems

Every percent problem in existence is one of three types:

**Type 1: Finding the percent of a number.**
"What is 25% of 80?"
Translation: $\frac{25}{100} \times 80 = 0.25 \times 80 = 20$.

**Type 2: Finding what percent one number is of another.**
"15 is what percent of 60?"
Translation: $\frac{15}{60} \times 100\% = 0.25 \times 100\% = 25\%$.

**Type 3: Finding the whole given a part and a percent.**
"12 is 30% of what number?"
Translation: $12 = 0.30 \times x$, so $x = \frac{12}{0.30} = 40$.

### 5.3.4 — The "Is-Of" Formula (The Universal Percent Equation)

The fundamental equation governing all percent relationships is:

$$\frac{\text{is}}{\text{of}} = \frac{\%}{100}$$

- The number after "is" goes in the numerator on the left.
- The number after "of" goes in the denominator on the left.
- The percent goes in the numerator on the right.
- 100 always goes in the denominator on the right.

**Example:** "What is 20% of 150?"
- "What" (unknown) = $is$
- 150 = $of$
- 20 = $\%$
- Equation: $\frac{x}{150} = \frac{20}{100} \to x = 30$.

**Example:** "8 is what percent of 32?"
- 8 = $is$
- 32 = $of$
- $x = \%$
- Equation: $\frac{8}{32} = \frac{x}{100} \to x = 25$.

**Example:** "18 is 12% of what number?"
- 18 = $is$
- $x = $of$
- 12 = $\%$
- Equation: $\frac{18}{x} = \frac{12}{100} \to 12x = 1800 \to x = 150$.

### 5.3.5 — The "Of" Version (When "Of" Appears Before the Percent)

When the word "of" appears before the percent, you multiply directly:

"20% of 150" = $0.20 \times 150 = 30$.

This is the "of" version—"of" means multiply. The "is" version uses the fraction form.

### 5.3.6 — Percent Increase and Decrease

**Percent increase formula:**

$$\text{Percent Increase} = \frac{\text{New Value} - \text{Old Value}}{\text{Old Value}} \times 100\%$$

**Percent decrease formula:**

$$\text{Percent Decrease} = \frac{\text{Old Value} - \text{New Value}}{\text{Old Value}} \times 100\%$$

Both formulas share the structure: $\frac{\text{Amount of change}}{\text{Original amount}} \times 100\%$.

> **Critical Rule:** The denominator is ALWAYS the **original** (starting) value, never the new value. This is one of the most common errors in percent change problems.

**Example:** A stock price rises from $40 to $50. The percent increase is:

$$\frac{50 - 40}{40} \times 100\% = \frac{10}{40} \times 100\% = 25\%$$

If the stock then falls from $50 back to $40, the percent decrease is:

$$\frac{50 - 40}{50} \times 100\% = \frac{10}{50} \times 100\% = 20\%$$

> **Key Insight:** A 25% increase followed by a 20% decrease does NOT return you to the starting value. The asymmetry arises because the base (denominator) changes. This is why percent changes are not reversible by simply applying the same percentage in the opposite direction.

### 5.3.7 — The Multiplier Method for Percent Changes

Instead of computing the change and adding/subtracting, you can use a **multiplier** (also called a **factor**):

- **Percent increase by $p\%$:** Multiply by $\left(1 + \frac{p}{100}\right)$
- **Percent decrease by $p\%$:** Multiply by $\left(1 - \frac{p}{100}\right)$

**Example:** Increase 80 by 15%.
Multiplier = $1 + \frac{15}{100} = 1.15$.
New value = $80 \times 1.15 = 92$.

**Example:** Decrease 200 by 30%.
Multiplier = $1 - \frac{30}{100} = 0.70$.
New value = $200 \times 0.70 = 140$.

This method is faster, less error-prone, and essential for handling successive percent changes.

### 5.3.8 — Successive Percent Changes

When multiple percent changes are applied in sequence, they are **multiplicative, NOT additive.**

**Example:** A price of $100 is increased by 20%, then decreased by 10%.

- After increase: $100 \times 1.20 = 120$
- After decrease: $120 \times 0.90 = 108$

The net effect is a single multiplier of $1.20 \times 0.90 = 1.08$, i.e., an 8% increase overall.

> **Common Trap:** Students often add the percentages: $+20\%$ and $-10\% = +10\%$. This is WRONG. The correct net change is $+8\%$ because the 10% decrease applies to the already-increased price of $120, not the original $100.

**General formula for successive changes:** If changes of $p\%$, $q\%$, $r\%$, ... are applied successively, the overall multiplier is:

$$\left(1 \pm \frac{p}{100}\right)\left(1 \pm \frac{q}{100}\right)\left(1 \pm \frac{r}{100}\right) \cdots$$

Use $+$ for increases and $-$ for decreases.

### 5.3.9 — Finding the Original Value After a Percent Change

If you know the final value and the percent change, you can work backward:

- If a value was **increased** by $p\%$ to reach $V$, the original was $\frac{V}{1 + p/100}$.
- If a value was **decreased** by $p\%$ to reach $V$, the original was $\frac{V}{1 - p/100}$.

**Example:** After a 15% discount, a jacket costs $85. What was the original price?

$$\text{Original} = \frac{85}{1 - 0.15} = \frac{85}{0.85} = 100$$

**Example:** After a 7% sales tax, a meal costs $21.40. What was the pre-tax price?

$$\text{Pre-tax} = \frac{21.40}{1 + 0.07} = \frac{21.40}{1.07} = 20.00$$

### 5.3.10 — Percent More Than / Percent Less Than

These phrases describe comparisons between two quantities:

- "$A$ is 40% more than $B$" means $A = B + 0.40B = 1.40B$.
- "$A$ is 25% less than $B$" means $A = B - 0.25B = 0.75B$.
- "$A$ is 60% of $B$" means $A = 0.60B$.

> **Subtle Distinction:** "40% more than" uses a multiplier of 1.40, while "40% of" uses a multiplier of 0.40. The word "more than" (or "less than") signals that you start with 100% and add/subtract.

### 5.3.11 — Mixture Problems

Mixture problems involve combining substances of different concentrations to produce a mixture of a desired concentration.

**The fundamental equation:**

$$(\%_1)(\text{amount}_1) + (\%_2)(\text{amount}_2) = (\%_{\text{mix}})(\text{total amount})$$

This equation states that the amount of "pure stuff" (the solute) in each component adds up to the amount of pure stuff in the mixture.

**Example framework:** How many liters of a 30% alcohol solution must be mixed with 5 liters of a 70% alcohol solution to produce a 40% alcohol solution?

Let $x$ = liters of 30% solution.

$$0.30x + 0.70(5) = 0.40(x + 5)$$

$$0.30x + 3.5 = 0.40x + 2.0$$

$$1.5 = 0.10x$$

$$x = 15 \text{ liters}$$

### 5.3.12 — Percents Greater Than 100%

Percents can exceed 100%. A value that is 150% of another is 1.5 times as large. A 200% increase means the new value is triple the original (original + 200% of original = 300% of original).

- $100\%$ of $x = x$
- $200\%$ of $x = 2x$
- $150\%$ increase means new value = $x + 1.5x = 2.5x$

---

## 5.4 — Rates: Ratios with Units

### 5.4.1 — Definition of a Rate

A **rate** is a special type of ratio that compares two quantities with **different units**. While a ratio of $3 : 5$ is unitless (comparing like quantities), a rate of 60 miles per hour compares distance to time.

**Common rates:**
- Speed: miles per hour (mph), meters per second (m/s)
- Density: grams per cubic centimeter (g/cm³)
- Price: dollars per pound ($/lb)
- Flow rate: gallons per minute (gpm)
- Population density: people per square mile

### 5.4.2 — Unit Rates

A **unit rate** is a rate with a denominator of 1 unit. For example, "60 miles per hour" is a unit rate—it tells you the distance covered in exactly 1 hour.

To find a unit rate, divide the numerator by the denominator:

$$\text{Unit rate} = \frac{\text{Quantity}}{\text{Number of units}}$$

**Example:** If 4.5 pounds of apples cost $9.45, the unit price is $\$9.45 \div 4.5 = \$2.10$ per pound.

### 5.4.3 — The Rate Equation: $D = RT$ (Distance = Rate $\times$ Time)

The most important rate equation in all of applied mathematics is:

$$\text{Distance} = \text{Rate} \times \text{Time}$$

Or equivalently: $d = rt$

From this, we derive:
- Rate = Distance / Time: $r = \frac{d}{t}$
- Time = Distance / Rate: $t = \frac{d}{r}$

**Unit consistency is paramount.** If rate is in miles per hour, time must be in hours and distance will be in miles. If time is given in minutes, you must convert to hours (or convert the rate to miles per minute).

**Example:** A car travels at 60 mph for 2.5 hours. Distance = $60 \times 2.5 = 150$ miles.

**Example:** A cyclist covers 45 km in 1.5 hours. Speed = $\frac{45}{1.5} = 30$ km/h.

**Example:** A plane flies 900 miles at 450 mph. Time = $\frac{900}{450} = 2$ hours.

### 5.4.4 — Average Speed: The Most Misunderstood Rate

**Average speed is NOT the average of the speeds.** It is defined as:

$$\text{Average Speed} = \frac{\text{Total Distance}}{\text{Total Time}}$$

This is a weighted average, not a simple arithmetic mean.

**Example:** A car travels 60 miles at 30 mph and then another 60 miles at 60 mph. What is the average speed for the entire trip?

- First segment: time = $\frac{60}{30} = 2$ hours
- Second segment: time = $\frac{60}{60} = 1$ hour
- Total distance = $60 + 60 = 120$ miles
- Total time = $2 + 1 = 3$ hours
- Average speed = $\frac{120}{3} = 40$ mph

The naive (wrong) answer would be $\frac{30 + 60}{2} = 45$ mph. This is wrong because the car spent more time traveling at the slower speed (2 hours at 30 mph vs. 1 hour at 60 mph), so the average is weighted toward 30 mph.

> **General Principle:** When equal distances are traveled at two different speeds $r_1$ and $r_2$, the average speed is the **harmonic mean**, not the arithmetic mean:

$$\text{Average speed} = \frac{2r_1 r_2}{r_1 + r_2}$$

For the example above: $\frac{2(30)(60)}{30 + 60} = \frac{3600}{90} = 40$ mph.

> **Contrast:** When equal **times** are spent at two different speeds, the average speed IS the arithmetic mean: $\frac{r_1 + r_2}{2}$. This is because equal time weights each speed equally.

### 5.4.5 — Rate Problems with Multiple Entities

**Approach problems (closing the gap):** When two objects move toward each other, their **relative speed** is the sum of their individual speeds. The distance between them closes at this combined rate.

**Example:** Two cars start 300 miles apart and drive toward each other. Car A travels at 55 mph, Car B at 65 mph. When do they meet?

Relative speed = $55 + 65 = 120$ mph.

Time to meet = $\frac{300}{120} = 2.5$ hours.

**Chase problems (catching up):** When one object follows another in the same direction, the relative speed is the **difference** of their speeds.

**Example:** Car A leaves at 9:00 AM traveling 50 mph. Car B leaves the same point at 10:00 AM traveling 70 mph. When does Car B catch Car A?

By 10:00 AM, Car A has a 50-mile head start.

Relative speed = $70 - 50 = 20$ mph.

Time to close 50-mile gap = $\frac{50}{20} = 2.5$ hours.

Car B catches Car A at 12:30 PM.

### 5.4.6 — Work Rates

Work problems are rate problems in disguise. The **work rate** of a person or machine is the fraction of the job completed per unit time.

**Key equation:**

$$\text{Work rate} = \frac{1 \text{ job}}{\text{Time to complete 1 job}}$$

If a person can complete a job in $t$ hours, their work rate is $\frac{1}{t}$ jobs per hour.

**Combined work rates:** When two or more entities work together, their individual rates add:

$$\frac{1}{t_{\text{total}}} = \frac{1}{t_1} + \frac{1}{t_2} + \frac{1}{t_3} + \cdots$$

**Example:** Pipe A can fill a tank in 4 hours. Pipe B can fill it in 6 hours. How long to fill the tank if both pipes are open?

$$\frac{1}{t} = \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{6} = \frac{3}{12} + \frac{2}{12} = \frac{5}{12}$$

$$t = \frac{12}{5} = 2.4 \text{ hours} = 2 \text{ hours } 24 \text{ minutes}$$

> **Common Error:** Students sometimes average the times: $\frac{4 + 6}{2} = 5$ hours. This is wrong. Working together should be FASTER than either individual, so the answer must be less than 4 hours.

### 5.4.7 — Converting Between Rate Units

Unit conversion for rates requires converting both the numerator unit and the denominator unit:

**Example:** Convert 60 miles per hour to feet per second.

- 1 mile = 5,280 feet
- 1 hour = 3,600 seconds

$$60 \frac{\text{miles}}{\text{hour}} \times \frac{5,280 \text{ feet}}{1 \text{ mile}} \times \frac{1 \text{ hour}}{3,600 \text{ seconds}} = \frac{60 \times 5,280}{3,600} \text{ ft/s} = 88 \text{ ft/s}$$

The technique of **dimensional analysis** (also called the factor-label method) involves multiplying by conversion factors arranged so that unwanted units cancel, leaving only the desired units.

---

## 5.5 — Factor of Change: The Deep Structure of Proportional Reasoning

### 5.5.1 — The Concept of Factor of Change

The **factor of change** is the multiplier that relates a new value to an original value. If a quantity changes from $V$ to $V'$, the factor of change is:

$$\text{Factor of change} = \frac{V'}{V}$$

- If the factor $> 1$, the quantity increased.
- If the factor $< 1$, the quantity decreased.
- If the factor $= 1$, the quantity is unchanged.

**Example:** A population grows from 5,000 to 6,000. The factor of change is $\frac{6,000}{5,000} = 1.2$, representing a 20% increase.

**Example:** A stock falls from $80 to $64. The factor of change is $\frac{64}{80} = 0.8$, representing a 20% decrease.

### 5.5.2 — The General Strategy for Factor-of-Change Problems

The factor-of-change approach is a powerful problem-solving framework that works whenever a problem describes how one or more input quantities change and asks about the effect on a related output quantity.

**The Five-Step Method:**

1. **Identify the formula** that relates the quantities involved.
2. **Identify which quantities are held constant** (these will drop out of the analysis).
3. **Replace each changing quantity with its factor of change** (a multiplier).
4. **Compute the overall factor of change** for the output quantity by multiplying the individual factors (raised to appropriate powers if the formula involves powers).
5. **Interpret the result:** A factor $> 1$ means increase; $< 1$ means decrease. Convert to percent change if needed.

### 5.5.3 — Linear Relationships: Direct Proportionality

If $y = kx$ (where $k$ is constant), then $y$ is directly proportional to $x$. If $x$ changes by a factor of $f$, then $y$ also changes by a factor of $f$.

**Example:** The cost $C$ of $n$ apples at $0.50 each is $C = 0.50n$. If the number of apples triples (factor = 3), the cost also triples (factor = 3).

**Example:** Distance $d = vt$ (velocity $\times$ time). If velocity is held constant and time doubles, distance doubles.

### 5.5.4 — Power Relationships: Squares and Square Roots

**Area of a square:** $A = s^2$

If the side length changes by a factor of $f$, the area changes by a factor of $f^2$.

**Example:** A square's side length increases by 10% (factor = 1.10). The new area is $(1.10)^2 = 1.21$ times the original, a **21% increase**.

> **Critical Insight:** A 10% increase in side length does NOT produce a 10% increase in area. Because area depends on the SQUARE of the side length, the factor of change for area is the SQUARE of the factor of change for the side.

**Conversely:** If the area of a square decreases to 64% of its original value (factor = 0.64), the side length changes by a factor of $\sqrt{0.64} = 0.80$, meaning the side length **decreased by 20%**.

**Area of a circle:** $A = \pi r^2$

If the radius changes by a factor of $f$, the area changes by a factor of $f^2$.

**Example:** If a circle's radius is halved (factor = 0.5), the area becomes $(0.5)^2 = 0.25$ of the original—a **75% decrease**.

### 5.5.5 — Volume Relationships: Cubes

**Volume of a cube:** $V = s^3$

If the side length changes by a factor of $f$, the volume changes by a factor of $f^3$.

**Example:** A cube's edge length doubles (factor = 2). The volume becomes $2^3 = 8$ times the original—a **700% increase** (or the volume is 800% of the original).

**Volume of a sphere:** $V = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$

If the radius changes by a factor of $f$, the volume changes by a factor of $f^3$.

**Example:** If a sphere's radius triples, its volume increases by a factor of $3^3 = 27$.

### 5.5.6 — Combined Factor-of-Change Problems

When a formula involves multiple variables, each changing by its own factor, the overall factor of change is the product of the individual factors (each raised to the power to which its variable is raised in the formula).

**Volume of a right circular cylinder:** $V = \pi r^2 h$

If the radius changes by a factor of $f_r$ and the height changes by a factor of $f_h$, the volume changes by a factor of $f_r^2 \times f_h$.

**Example:** A cylinder's radius decreases by 20% (factor = 0.80) and its height increases by 10% (factor = 1.10). The new volume is:

$$(0.80)^2(1.10) = 0.64 \times 1.10 = 0.704$$

times the original. This is a **29.6% decrease** in volume.

**Volume of a cone:** $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$

Same analysis as the cylinder—the constant $\frac{1}{3}\pi$ doesn't affect the factor of change.

**Electric power in a resistive circuit:** $P = \frac{V^2}{R}$

If voltage changes by a factor of $f_V$ and resistance is constant, power changes by a factor of $f_V^2$.

**Example:** If voltage is halved (factor = 0.5), power drops to $(0.5)^2 = 0.25$ of the original—a **75% decrease**. This is why high-voltage transmission lines are used: to deliver the same power at higher voltage (and lower current), resistive losses ($I^2R$) are dramatically reduced.

### 5.5.7 — Inverse Relationships in Factor-of-Change

If $y = \frac{k}{x}$ (inverse proportionality), then when $x$ changes by a factor of $f$, $y$ changes by a factor of $\frac{1}{f}$.

**Example:** For a fixed distance, time = distance/speed. If speed doubles (factor = 2), time is halved (factor = $\frac{1}{2}$).

**Example:** Boyle's Law: $PV = k$ (constant temperature). If pressure increases by a factor of 3, volume decreases by a factor of $\frac{1}{3}$.

### 5.5.8 — Scale Factor in Similar Figures

**Scale factor** is the ratio of corresponding lengths in two similar figures. If the scale factor from Figure A to Figure B is $k$:

- **Length ratio** = $k$
- **Perimeter ratio** = $k$ (perimeter is a linear measure)
- **Area ratio** = $k^2$ (area is a two-dimensional measure)
- **Volume ratio** = $k^3$ (volume is a three-dimensional measure)

**Example:** Two similar triangles have a scale factor of $3 : 5$ (from the smaller to the larger).
- The ratio of their perimeters is $3 : 5$.
- The ratio of their areas is $9 : 25$.
- If the area of the smaller triangle is 18 cm², the area of the larger is $18 \times \frac{25}{9} = 50$ cm².

> **Common Error:** Students often assume that if the scale factor for length is $k$, the area scale factor is also $k$. It is $k^2$. This error is so common that it appears on virtually every standardized test.

### 5.5.9 — Working Backward from Area/Volume Ratios to Length Ratios

If you know the ratio of areas of two similar figures, the scale factor for lengths is the **square root** of the area ratio.

**Example:** The area of a larger square is 144% of the area of a smaller square. What is the scale factor for side lengths?

Area ratio = 1.44. Scale factor = $\sqrt{1.44} = 1.2$. The larger square's side is 1.2 times the smaller's—a 20% increase.

If the area ratio is $64\% = 0.64$, the scale factor is $\sqrt{0.64} = 0.80$, meaning the side length decreased by 20%.

Similarly, for volume: if the volume ratio is $k$, the length ratio is $\sqrt[3]{k}$ (the cube root).

---

## 5.6 — Advanced Ratio and Proportion Tactics

### 5.6.1 — The "Pick Numbers" Strategy

When a problem gives only ratios and percentages without absolute values, you can **assign convenient values** to the quantities and solve numerically. This converts an abstract proportional problem into a concrete arithmetic problem.

**Example:** "The ratio of boys to girls in a class is $3 : 5$. If there are 24 more girls than boys, how many students are in the class?"

Pick numbers consistent with the ratio: let boys = $3x$ and girls = $5x$.
The difference is $5x - 3x = 2x = 24$, so $x = 12$.
Boys = 36, girls = 60, total = 96.

This is the multiplier method in action, but the "pick numbers" philosophy extends further: you can pick actual numbers (not just algebraic expressions) when the answer choices are also in ratio or percent form.

### 5.6.2 — Using the Answer Choices (Backsolving)

When the answer choices are specific numbers and the problem involves ratios or percents, you can test each answer choice to see which one satisfies the conditions. Start with choice C (the middle value) to determine whether you need a larger or smaller value.

### 5.6.3 — Converting Between Fractions, Decimals, and Percents Fluency

The following conversions should be automatic:

| Fraction | Decimal | Percent |
|---|---|---|
| $\frac{1}{2}$ | 0.50 | 50% |
| $\frac{1}{3}$ | 0.333... | 33.33% |
| $\frac{1}{4}$ | 0.25 | 25% |
| $\frac{1}{5}$ | 0.20 | 20% |
| $\frac{1}{6}$ | 0.1667 | 16.67% |
| $\frac{1}{8}$ | 0.125 | 12.5% |
| $\frac{1}{10}$ | 0.10 | 10% |
| $\frac{2}{3}$ | 0.666... | 66.67% |
| $\frac{3}{4}$ | 0.75 | 75% |
| $\frac{3}{5}$ | 0.60 | 60% |
| $\frac{4}{5}$ | 0.80 | 80% |

Memorizing these conversions saves enormous time on tests and reduces computational errors.

### 5.6.4 — Percent-Fraction Equivalents for Estimation

Some percent-fraction relationships are particularly useful for estimation:

- $12.5\% = \frac{1}{8}$ → to find 12.5% of a number, divide by 8
- $33.33\% = \frac{1}{3}$ → to find 33.33% of a number, divide by 3
- $66.67\% = \frac{2}{3}$ → to find 66.67% of a number, divide by 3 and multiply by 2
- $75\% = \frac{3}{4}$ → to find 75% of a number, divide by 4 and multiply by 3
- $20\% = \frac{1}{5}$ → to find 20% of a number, divide by 5
- $10\% = \frac{1}{10}$ → to find 10% of a number, divide by 10

### 5.6.5 — The "10% and 1%" Technique

To find any percent of a number mentally:

1. Find 10% by moving the decimal one place left.
2. Find 1% by moving the decimal two places left.
3. Combine these building blocks.

**Example:** Find 23% of 400.
- 10% of 400 = 40
- 20% of 400 = 80
- 1% of 400 = 4
- 3% of 400 = 12
- 23% of 400 = $80 + 12 = 92$

**Example:** Find 17.5% of 800.
- 10% = 80
- 5% = 40 (half of 10%)
- 2.5% = 20 (half of 5%)
- 17.5% = $80 + 40 + 20 = 140$

### 5.6.6 — Weighted Averages and Ratios

When two groups with different averages are combined, the overall average is a **weighted average** based on the sizes of the groups.

$$\text{Weighted Average} = \frac{n_1 \cdot \bar{x}_1 + n_2 \cdot \bar{x}_2}{n_1 + n_2}$$

This can be solved using ratios. If the ratio of group sizes is $a : b$, the weighted average divides the interval between the two group means in the ratio $b : *a*$ (the **inverse** ratio).

**Example:** Class A has 20 students with a mean score of 80. Class B has 30 students with a mean score of 90. What is the overall mean?

The ratio of sizes is $20 : 30 = 2 : 3$. The overall mean divides the interval from 80 to 90 in the ratio $3 : 2$ (inverse of $2 : 3$).

The interval is 10 points. Dividing in ratio $3 : 2$ gives 6 points and 4 points.
Overall mean = $80 + 6 = 86$ (or equivalently, $90 - 4 = 86$).

**Verification:** $\frac{20 \times 80 + 30 \times 90}{50} = \frac{1600 + 2700}{50} = \frac{4300}{50} = 86$.

---

## 5.7 — Comprehensive Problem-Solving Frameworks

### 5.7.1 — The "Before and After" Framework

Many percent and ratio problems describe a situation before and after a change. The systematic approach:

1. **Define variables** for the "before" quantities.
2. **Express the "after" quantities** in terms of the "before" quantities and the given changes.
3. **Set up equations** based on the relationships described.
4. **Solve** for the unknowns.

**Example framework:** "A company's workforce is 60% male. After hiring 20 additional female workers, the workforce is 50% male. How many employees are there now?"

Let $x$ = original total employees.
Original males = $0.60x$, original females = $0.40x$.
After hiring: males = $0.60x$ (unchanged), total = $x + 20$.
New male percentage: $\frac{0.60x}{x + 20} = 0.50$.

$$0.60x = 0.50x + 10$$

$$0.10x = 10$$

$$x = 100 \text{ (original)}$$

New total = 120.

### 5.7.2 — The "Constant Total" vs. "Constant Part" Distinction

In mixture and concentration problems, it is essential to identify what remains constant:

- **Adding/removing one component:** The total changes, but the amount of the other component stays the same.
- **Evaporating solvent:** The amount of solute stays the same; the total decreases.
- **Adding solute:** The amount of solvent stays the same; the total increases.

**Example:** "How much water must be evaporated from 40 liters of a 20% salt solution to produce a 50% salt solution?"

The amount of salt is constant: $0.20 \times 40 = 8$ liters of salt.
After evaporation, 8 liters of salt = 50% of the new total.
New total = $\frac{8}{0.50} = 16$ liters.
Water evaporated = $40 - 16 = 24$ liters.

### 5.7.3 — The "Percent of" vs. "Percent Greater Than" Trap

These two constructions mean different things:

- "$A$ is 150% **of** $B$" → $A = 1.5 \times B$
- "$A$ is 150% **greater than** $B$" → $A = B + 1.5 \times B = 2.5 \times B$

The phrase "greater than" (or "less than") signals that you start with the base (100%) and add/subtract the percent.

Similarly:
- "$A$ is 40% **of** $B$" → $A = 0.40 \times B$
- "$A$ is 40% **less than** $B$" → $A = B - 0.40 \times B = 0.60 \times B$

### 5.7.4 — The "Percent More/Less Than" Reversal

If $A$ is $p\%$ more than $B$, then $B$ is NOT $p\%$ less than $A$. The base has changed.

**Example:** $A = 150$, $B = 100$. $A$ is 50% more than $B$. But $B$ is $\frac{150 - 100}{150} = 33.33\%$ less than $A$.

The general formula: If $A$ is $p\%$ more than $B$, then $B$ is $\frac{p}{100 + p} \times 100\%$ less than $A$.

If $A$ is $p\%$ less than $B$, then $B$ is $\frac{p}{100 - p} \times 100\%$ more than $A$.

### 5.7.5 — Ratio Problems with Changing Quantities

When a quantity is transferred between two groups, or when quantities change while a ratio shifts, the key is to identify what (if anything) remains constant.

**Type 1: Constant total.** Items are redistributed between groups, but the total stays the same.

**Type 2: Constant difference.** Quantities are added/subtracted equally from both groups, so the difference stays the same.

**Type 3: One quantity changes while the other stays constant.**

Each type requires a different algebraic setup. The first step is always to identify the invariant.

---

## 5.8 — Rates in Context: Advanced Applications

### 5.8.1 — Fuel Efficiency and Consumption Rates

Fuel efficiency (miles per gallon) and fuel consumption (gallons per mile) are **reciprocal rates**. A car that gets 30 mpg consumes $\frac{1}{30}$ gallons per mile.

If fuel efficiency doubles (factor = 2), fuel consumption per mile is halved (factor = $\frac{1}{2}$).

### 5.8.2 — Density as a Rate

Density = Mass / Volume. It is a rate relating mass to volume.

If the volume of an object changes while mass stays constant, density changes inversely. If you compress a gas to half its volume (factor = $\frac{1}{2}$) without changing its mass, the density doubles (factor = 2).

### 5.8.3 — Population Growth as a Rate

Population growth can be modeled as a rate: the change in population per unit time. In simple models, the growth rate is proportional to the current population, leading to exponential growth. In the context of this chapter, we focus on the proportional aspects:

If a population grows by $r\%$ per year, the multiplier per year is $\left(1 + \frac{r}{100}\right)$. After $t$ years, the population is multiplied by $\left(1 + \frac{r}{100}\right)^t$.

### 5.8.4 — Concentration as a Rate

Concentration = Amount of solute / Amount of solution. It is a rate (or ratio) that can be expressed as a percent, a fraction, or in units like molarity (moles per liter).

When solutions are mixed, the fundamental equation is:

$$C_1 V_1 + C_2 V_2 = C_{\text{mix}} V_{\text{mix}}$$

where $C$ is concentration and $V$ is volume. This is the same structure as the mixture equation from Section 5.3.11.

### 5.8.5 — Angular Rates

Rotational speed can be measured in degrees per second, radians per second, or revolutions per minute (rpm). Conversions between these units use the facts that 1 revolution = $360^\circ = 2\pi$ radians.

**Example:** A wheel rotates at 120 rpm. Its angular speed in degrees per second is:

$$120 \frac{\text{rev}}{\text{min}} \times \frac{360^\circ}{1 \text{ rev}} \times \frac{1 \text{ min}}{60 \text{ sec}} = 720^\circ/\text{sec}$$

---

## 5.9 — Factor-of-Change Mastery: Worked Conceptual Analyses

### 5.9.1 — The Square Area Paradigm (Deep Dive)

This is the canonical factor-of-change problem type and deserves thorough analysis.

**Statement:** If the side length of a square increases by $p\%$, by what percent does the area increase?

**Analysis:**
Let original side = $s$. Original area = $s^2$.
New side = $s\left(1 + \frac{p}{100}\right)$.
New area = $s^2\left(1 + \frac{p}{100}\right)^2 = s^2\left(1 + \frac{2p}{100} + \left(\frac{p}{100}\right)^2\right)$.

Percent increase = $\left[\frac{2p}{100} + \left(\frac{p}{100}\right)^2\right] \times 100\% = \left(2p + \frac{p^2}{100}\right)\%$.

**For $p = 10\%$:** Increase = $20 + 1 = 21\%$.
**For $p = 20\%$:** Increase = $40 + 4 = 44\%$.
**For $p = 50\%$:** Increase = $100 + 25 = 125\%$.
**For $p = 100\%$:** Increase = $200 + 100 = 300\%$ (area quadruples).

The formula $2p + \frac{p^2}{100}$ shows that the percent increase in area has a linear term ($2p$) and a quadratic term ($\frac{p^2}{100}$). For small $p$, the quadratic term is negligible, and the increase is approximately $2p\%$. For large $p$, the quadratic term becomes significant.

**Reverse problem:** If the area of a square increases by $p\%$, by what percent does the side length increase?

New area = $s^2\left(1 + \frac{p}{100}\right)$.
New side = $s\sqrt{1 + \frac{p}{100}}$.

Percent increase in side = $\left[\sqrt{1 + \frac{p}{100}} - 1\right] \times 100\%$.

**For $p = 44\%$:** Side increase = $[\sqrt{1.44} - 1] \times 100\% = [1.2 - 1] \times 100\% = 20\%$.

### 5.9.2 — The Circle Area Paradigm

Identical analysis to the square, since $A = \pi r^2$ depends on the square of the radius.

If the circumference of a circle increases by $p\%$, by what percent does the area increase?

Since $C = 2\pi r$, the circumference is proportional to $r$. If $C$ increases by $p\%$, $r$ also increases by $p\%$. Therefore, the area increases by the same percent as if the radius increased by $p\%$: $2p + \frac{p^2}{100}\%$.

### 5.9.3 — The Triangle Area Paradigm (Scale Factor)

For similar triangles, the area ratio equals the square of the side ratio. This was covered in Section 5.5.8, but let's extend it:

**Example:** In two similar triangles, the ratio of corresponding sides is $5 : 7$. The area of the larger triangle is 98 cm². Find the area of the smaller triangle.

Area ratio = $5^2 : 7^2 = 25 : 49$.
Area of smaller = $98 \times \frac{25}{49} = 50$ cm².

### 5.9.4 — The Cylinder Volume Paradigm
